# PBE Address Book — Decision Log

ADR-style record of decisions made during planning: what was decided, why, and when. The compact read-first artifact that lets later sessions honor earlier conclusions. See `history/DESIGN-HISTORY.md` for the roadmap and session history.

*This decision log graduated from the planning vault into the repo at the Session-6c close-out (2026-06-07); its seed-document citations were stripped so it stands alone, and illustrative data uses the fake exemplar "James Smyth '84 (#5247)."*

---

## Session 1 — Scope & Architecture Foundations (2026-06-02)

### D1 — Documentation homes: process notes in the vault, deliverables in the repo
Working/process artifacts (this decision log, the index, open questions, optimizations) live in the Obsidian vault; the delivered documents (PRD, schema, engineering design, API spec, coding plan, user manual) live in the `pbe-address-book/` code repo and are checked in with the code.
**Why:** Keeps rationale and exploration in the vault (where wikilinks resolve and notes are browsable) while the finished design ships with the code, per the global "docs ship with code" rule. Supersedes the original plan of authoring everything in the vault and migrating later.

*Close-out note (Session 6c): at the end of planning, these process docs — this decision log and `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS.md` — graduated into the repo under `docs/initial-build/`, with the seed notes preserved as `history/SEED.md` and the planning session log distilled into `history/DESIGN-HISTORY.md`. See `docs/README.md`.*

### D2 — Build from scratch rather than on Django
Book is a custom application, not built on a server-centric framework such as Django.
**Why:** The app's defining trait is client-side ownership of the data (bulk download, all query/sort/filter local), which drains most of Django's value (ORM, admin, server rendering, forms). Fitting Django to a serverless + tiny-blob + SPA shape would require brittle add-ons (SPA frontend, Litestream-style DB persistence, cold-start mitigations) — heavyweight cost without the benefit. AI-assisted development removes the time-to-first-app advantage that frameworks historically provided.

### D3 — TypeScript end-to-end, formatted and linted with Biome
Both frontend and backend are TypeScript, sharing a single `Profile` type. Biome handles formatting and linting, enforced by format-on-save, a pre-commit hook, and a CI check.
**Why:** A single shared record type defined once and imported on both sides means the compiler catches any front/back drift — valuable for an app whose job is shuttling one record shape back and forth. Biome is one fast tool replacing the Prettier + ESLint pair, fitting the minimal-dependency preference; structural enforcement makes formatting non-optional rather than a matter of discipline.

### D4 — SPA bulk-download architecture
The browser downloads the dataset and performs all search, filter, sort, and display locally; the backend is a thin serverless API.
**Why:** At < ~2,000 tiny records the dataset fits trivially in browser memory, making client-side operations instant and a server-side query/pagination API unnecessary over-building. Yields snappy UX, a minimal/cheap backend, and a clean serverless + document-store fit. Rejected the conventional server-side-query alternative as the wrong fit for this scale.

### D5 — Server-side privacy projection (field-level visibility enforced on the backend)
The backend projects every response down to the fields the requesting user is authorized to see (by role and per-record privacy flags). The frontend never receives data the user may not see.
**Why:** A frontend-only privacy filter is security theater — hidden fields would still be readable in the network response or DevTools. "Private" only becomes real when enforcement lives on the server. Resolves the privacy-leak risk a frontend-only filter would otherwise leave open.

*Later updated by: D16 (the four-class field-visibility taxonomy extends this projection).*

### D6 — Download the full permitted record set (no hybrid/partial payload)
The bulk download contains every field the user is permitted to see for every record — not a list-only projection with lazy-fetched detail.
**Why:** Client-side filtering and sorting can touch any field, even ones not currently displayed as a column, so the fields must be present locally regardless of display. A hybrid would force a server round-trip whenever a user filtered on a non-downloaded field, defeating instant search. Full download also makes column-switching and filtering instant. (The only lazy-loaded item is the headshot.)

### D7 — Firestore as system of record; in-memory cache; single blob as wire/cache format
Firestore (document per profile) is the durable system of record. The backend caches the dataset in memory and serves bulk downloads from cache; the "single JSON document" lives on the wire and in the client/cache, not as the storage format. Writes are written through to Firestore synchronously.
**Why:** Firestore provides durability, atomic per-record writes, and optimistic-concurrency primitives for free, removing the in-memory-system-of-record reliability risk (instances can crash or be lost without a clean shutdown signal, so memory must only ever be a disposable cache). Once per-user projection is required (D5) the backend already transforms data per request, so the single-blob's "serve a static file" simplicity was moot — its concurrency/coherence footguns weren't worth keeping. Per-document read cost is immaterial at this scale and is further nullified by the cache (client bulk-loads cost zero Firestore reads).

### D8 — Headshots, thumbnails, and backups in Google Cloud Storage (versioned)
Binary assets and database backups live in GCS objects with object versioning enabled, not in Firestore.
**Why:** Blobs do not belong in a document database (size limits, cost). GCS serves them as cacheable objects; object versioning gives near-free point-in-time history that doubles as part of the backup story.

*Later updated by: D94 (superseded headshot/thumbnail versions purged after a 3-month window).*

### D9 — Thumbnails: lazy/prefetch-loaded, CDN-served, precomputed — not bundled, not dropped
Thumbnails stay (visual richness) but are not bundled into the JSON payload. They are precomputed on headshot upload, stored as individually addressable immutable cacheable objects, served from the CDN edge (not the app backend), and loaded with a viewport lookahead margin plus optional idle-time prefetch of the current result set in scroll order.
**Why:** Bundling ~2,000 images would bloat and block the dataset payload; dropping them loses wanted richness. Lazy + prefetch loading fetches only what's needed (a few hundred KB on demand) and caches it; CDN edge serving plus HTTP/2 multiplexing keep it fast and keep per-image requests off the app backend. The architecture (addressable cacheable URLs) enables any prefetch policy without data-model changes, so exact aggressiveness is a Session 4 tuning knob.

*Later updated by: D42 (concretizes the deferred prefetch/lookahead policy), D94 (superseded thumbnail versions purged after 3 months), D126 (thumbnails are served by the app from a private bucket, not the CDN edge — the prefetch architecture is unchanged).*

### D10 — Frontend React + Vite; backend Node + TS on Cloud Run; static SPA on Firebase Hosting/CDN
Frontend is React + TypeScript built with Vite, a pure client SPA with no SSR/meta-framework. Backend is Node + TypeScript on Google Cloud Run. The static SPA bundle is served from Firebase Hosting / CDN.
**Why:** React has the most training data (better AI-assisted codegen) and a component model that fits a small four-page app; a meta-framework like Next.js exists for SSR/server data-fetching this authed bulk-download SPA does not need, so plain Vite stays lean. Static hosting + serverless API are cheap-to-free at this scale.

### D11 — Scope: MVP vs. deferred
MVP delivers the full working address book. Deferred: report page; Ghost content search and the "Search News" link; the Big Brother *graph browser* (data entry stays in MVP); Mailman integration; external API; and automated MITAA exchange (manual CSV import covers it for MVP). Ghost membership sync stays in MVP at least for the initial pre-launch load; its conflict policy is a Session 5 design.
**Why:** Concentrate first-release planning and build effort on the core; everything tagged "nice-to-have" or "future work" is deferred without being rejected.

*Later updated by: D27 (the Linter gets a scoped first-party path), D60 (Mailman integration deferred, replacement directions logged).*

---

## Session 2 — Data Model / Profile Schema (2026-06-02)

Deliverable: `DATABASE-SCHEMA.md` in the repo. Resolves all six Session-2 open questions.

### D12 — Three Firestore collections; private state split out of profiles
Persistent data lives in three collections — `profiles` (directory data), `users` (per-user private state: `role`, `stars`), `majors` (controlled vocabulary) — plus image objects in GCS. The `users` document is keyed by the same Constitution ID as the profile.
**Why:** Stars and role are private per-user state that no other brother should receive; keeping them out of the `profiles` collection keeps them out of the bulk-download payload and keeps the server-side privacy projection (D5) operating over directory data only.

### D13 — Class year is `number | null` (null = unknown)
`classYear` is a 4-digit number or `null`; validation range `1890 … currentYear + 6`. Displayed `'84`, or `'??`/omitted when null.
**Why:** A real `null` forces every TypeScript consumer to handle the unknown case explicitly, rather than a magic sentinel (`0`/`"UNKN"`) that would silently sort or format wrong.

*Later updated by: R19 (composite §12 — client validation tolerance widened to currentYear+7; the server hard gate stays +6).*

### D14 — Majors as a Firestore controlled-vocabulary collection of codes
`majors` is one document per MIT course code (`{ code, displayName, active }`). Profiles store an array of codes (primary first; supports double majors); display names are derived by in-memory lookup. Retired courses carry `active: false` so historical majors stay valid.
**Why:** Codes give clean searchability (e.g. "everyone in Course 6") that free text would wreck; the dataset is in browser memory, so the lookup is an O(1) map hit, not a relational join. Firestore (not hard-coding) lets the list change without a code release; storing codes keeps profile JSON compact and gives a single source of truth for names.

*Later updated by: D69 (the `majors` vocabulary is maintained via a checked-in source file + idempotent seed/reconcile script; runtime curation UI deferred).*

### D15 — Canonical Name constructed, with derived ambiguity and ID-only disambiguation
Canonical Name (`First Last 'YY`) is computed by one shared function used by Book and the Linter, never stored. Ambiguity is derived at load time via an in-memory frequency map keyed on the *displayed* identity `(firstName, lastName, two-digit-yearToken)`; collisions append the Constitution ID (`James Smyth '84 (#5247)`). Suffixes are folded into `fullLegalName`; there is no separate suffix field and no stored ambiguity flag.
**Why:** The middle initial is unusable (many lack one) and Sr./Jr. only distinguishes father/son, who can never share a class year and so never collide — only the ID separates real (unrelated) collisions. Deriving ambiguity from the in-memory dataset is free and always correct; a stored flag would go stale on every edit/delete (same principle as deriving little-brothers from the graph).

*Later updated by: D58 (Book serves the constructed canonical name so the Linter shares it across the language boundary).*

### D16 — Field-visibility taxonomy enforced by server projection
Every field has one of four visibility classes: **public** (all brothers), **toggle** (peer-visible only if the owner's share flag is true — `shareEmail`→email/altEmail, `sharePhone`, `shareAddress`, `shareEmergency`, all default true), **restricted** (owner/manager/admin only, no toggle — the privacy flags, `allowNewsletterEmail`, `allowShareWithMITAA`, `lastVerifiedDate`, `lastModified`), and **private** (the `users` collection — `role`, `stars`). Extends D5.
**Why:** `allowNewsletterEmail`/`allowShareWithMITAA` are sensitive (a brother may not want peers to know their choices) but managers/admins need them as directory columns — so they must arrive in the projected bulk download for those roles. Restricted-class projection hides them from peers without building a second projection/bulk-download path over `users`. `shareEmergency` was added so emergency-contact info is independently hideable. Mailman subscription fields are excluded from MVP (integration deferred, D11).

*Later updated by: D19 (three-tier read projection; supersedes "managers see toggle/restricted like admins"), D93 (third-party-data share toggles — `shareEmergency` defaults off, `shareSpousePartner` added).*

### D17 — Image assets in GCS: versioned, immutable, WEBP
Headshots and thumbnails are GCS objects at `headshots/{id}/{version}.webp` and `thumbnails/{id}/{version}.webp`; the profile holds only `hasHeadshot` and `headshotVersion`. Square 1:1, headshot 512×512, thumbnail 96×96; uploads accept JPEG/PNG, stored/served as WEBP; thumbnail derived from the client-cropped headshot; GCS object versioning enabled.
**Why:** `headshotVersion` in the path makes every upload a fresh, immutable, indefinitely-cacheable URL whose cache busts by changing (D9); object versioning (D8) gives near-free history. A single 1:1 ratio serves grid thumbnail, profile photo, and newsletter byline consistently.

*Later updated by: D94 (superseded headshot/thumbnail versions purged after 3 months).*

### D18 — `address.country` validated as ISO 3166-1 alpha-2 from a bundled constant
Country stores the ISO 3166-1 alpha-2 code (e.g. `GB`, `US`, `CN`, `TW`) and derives the display name from a bundled standard list; `stateProvince` stays free text; the structured address mirrors MITAA's fields. Broader physical-address validation/autocomplete is deferred (added to PRD future work).
**Why:** Coded countries give a pick-list, unambiguous identity (e.g. CN vs. TW), clean MITAA exchange, and enable future geographic analytics (a brothers-by-country map). Unlike majors, the ISO list is a fixed external standard that essentially never changes, so it is bundled in the build rather than made a fourth Firestore collection — org-specific mutable data goes in Firestore, stable external standards get bundled.

*Later updated by: D37 (US/CA state-province controlled vocabulary, extending this principle to subdivisions).*

---

## Session 3 — Auth, Permissions & API (2026-06-03)

Deliverables: `ENGINEERING-DESIGN.md` §2 (plus §1.5 and §4), the new `API-SPEC.md`, `PRD.md` §4, and the `DATABASE-SCHEMA.md` amendments described in D19. Resolves all five Session-3 open questions.

### D19 — Role/capability matrix and a three-tier read projection (amends D16)
Three roles live in `users`: **brother**, **manager**, **admin**. The manager/admin line is drawn on *actions*, not raw visibility: managers maintain the directory (edit any visible field, set deceased status, export CSV, verify) while delete, add-brother, bulk-CSV import, role changes, Ghost sync, backup/restore, and changing a brother's privacy/consent settings are admin-only. Add-Brother is admin-only because the Constitution roster is pre-loaded, so additions are rare and warrant admin attention. The read model narrows D16 to three projections: **toggle-class** contact values are visible to the owner and admins always, but to ordinary brothers *and managers* only when the owner's share flag is on (managers no longer see through an off-toggle); **restricted-class** flags/preferences/dates (the share-toggles, `allowNewsletterEmail`, `allowShareWithMITAA`, `lastVerifiedDate`, `verifiedBy`, `lastModified`) stay visible to owner/manager/admin but are read-only for managers; only admins see the contact values hidden behind an off-toggle. A new `verifiedBy` field was added, and verification was opened to owner/manager/admin with role-specific on-screen caution copy (refined by D28 — verification is coupled to content edits).
**Why:** The housekeeper-and-homeowner model — a manager tidies what is in the open but does not open private drawers or knock down walls; the admin (homeowner) can do anything. Managers can see *that* a field is private and see verification dates (so they can coach brothers and chase stale records) without seeing the protected values or being able to override a brother's consent — those belong to the brother or the admin. `verifiedBy` records provenance (self-attested vs. staff-attested), which is valuable even though managers are trusted to verify; the differentiated button copy guards against careless verification. This supersedes D16's "managers see toggle/restricted like admins" and its rationale for keeping the consent flags in `profiles` (they are still in `profiles`, but now for admins, not managers).

*Later updated by: D28 (verification coupled to content edits — refines the verify capability).*

### D20 — Ghost auth bridge via a Handlebars page and Ghost's member JWT
Authentication bridges through `pbe400.org/book` — a custom Handlebars template mapped in `routes.yaml`. A logged-in member's browser fetches a Ghost-signed JWT from the same-origin `/members/api/session`; the page redirects to `book.pbe400.org/auth/callback` with the token in the URL **fragment**; Book's backend verifies it against Ghost's JWKS (`/members/.well-known/jwks.json`) and establishes its own session. Unauthenticated visitors are sent to Ghost's Portal sign-in (magic link) and bounced back. An email that matches no Book profile is **denied with a "contact an administrator" message**; a first successful match creates the `users` doc with role `brother`.
**Why:** Ghost Pro exposes no server config (Nginx) or CORS control, ruling out a subdirectory proxy, a cookie-rewrite, or a direct cross-origin members-API call — but it does allow custom templates and `routes.yaml`, and Ghost already mints exactly the signed token we need. JWKS verification is a true cryptographic check, not a trust-the-client handoff. The fragment keeps the token out of logs, history, and `Referer`. Deny-and-contact-admin handles both new initiates and the ~70 historical unidentified Ghost addresses, and funnels each unknown to an admin for identification/linking.

*Later updated by: D104 (JWT alg-pin + single-use callback nonce + redirect allowlist), D105 (Ghost single-point-of-compromise dependency closed where closable, documented otherwise).*

### D21 — Pluggable identity via an `IdentityProvider` seam
The backend isolates authentication behind a single `IdentityProvider` interface (`verify(rawToken) → { email }`), implemented by `GhostIdentityProvider`. Email→user resolution, role lookup, and Book's session are all provider-agnostic.
**Why:** Realizes the project goal of swappable auth set out from the start. A future move to (e.g.) Google OIDC is a new provider plus a new bridge entry point, leaving session and authorization untouched. Book keeps its own role table regardless of provider.

### D22 — Book session: session cookie plus a 4-hour server-side lifetime cap
Book issues its own session cookie on `book.pbe400.org` (independent of Ghost), as a session cookie *and* with a 4-hour server-side expiry; on lapse, the next request re-bounces through the bridge (silent if still a Ghost member, denied if removed). Cookie is `HttpOnly`, `Secure`, `SameSite=Strict`.
**Why:** A session cookie alone is a soft guarantee (session-restore preserves it; an always-open browser never re-auths), so the server-side cap is what bounds the revocation window and ensures a brother removed from Ghost loses Book access promptly. Four hours balances that against re-auth friction — and re-auth is a silent redirect, not a fresh magic-link round-trip.

*Later updated by: D109 (non-destructive 401/403 recovery for the absolute 4-hour cap — preserve the in-progress edit, re-auth via a child window).*

### D23 — Media authentication via Cloud CDN signed cookies (not signed URLs)
CDN-served headshots/thumbnails are gated by a single Cloud CDN signed cookie issued at session establishment, scoped to the image prefix, with a TTL equal to the session and reissued with it. The grant is coarse (any authenticated brother may fetch any image). Images are served under the Book origin (`/img/*`) to keep the cookie host-only and same-site; reads never touch the app backend, writes go through an authenticated upload endpoint.
**Why:** Signed URLs would force a backend round-trip per image (killing the lazy/prefetch grid) or leak a signing key to the client, and would fight the immutable-cacheable-URL model (D9/D17). A signed cookie grants the whole prefix and rides along automatically. Coarse granularity is correct because photos carry no per-brother privacy (confirmed: any brother may see any brother's photos).

*Later updated by: D94 (headshot-version purge), D107 (host-only cookie discipline extended to the session cookie), D109 (image-`403` re-auth-and-retry on cap lapse), D126 (the signed-cookie + Cloud-CDN + load-balancer mechanism is replaced by app-served images from a private bucket; the coarse members-only grant stands).*

### D24 — REST API surface and conventions
All backend endpoints live under `/api/*` (distinct from SPA client routes and the `/img/*` CDN path), session-authenticated, JSON, HTTPS. Resources: `POST /api/auth/session`, `GET /api/me`; `GET /api/profiles` (bulk, projected, no image bytes, from the backend cache), `GET/POST/PATCH/DELETE /api/profiles[/{id}]`, `POST /api/profiles/{id}/verify`; `PUT/DELETE /api/profiles/{id}/headshot` and thumbnail regenerate; `PUT/DELETE /api/me/stars/{id}`; `PUT /api/users/{id}/role`; reserved `/api/admin/*`. Profile edits are **PATCH only** (no PUT); `POST /api/profiles` is one atomic create carrying the full record with a **caller-supplied** Constitution ID (409 on duplicate); the headshot is a singleton sub-resource updated by **PUT** (create-or-replace); there is **no** image-read endpoint (CDN direct), **no** logout endpoint, and **no** multi-ID GET filter. Full contract in `API-SPEC.md`.
**Why:** The `/api` prefix keeps data endpoints cleanly separable in the load-balancer URL map from SPA routes and image objects. PATCH-only fits partial saves. The Constitution ID is a real-world number from the physical signature page, never auto-assigned. An atomic full-record POST avoids empty-record windows. PUT to a fixed singleton URL is the correct create-or-replace verb. Image reads are off-backend (D9); logout is unnecessary given browser-close + the 4-hour cap; the multi-ID/query filter belongs to the deferred external API.

*Later updated by: D82 (a separate `GET /api/me` self-fetch splits the read), D95 (adds a Sign-out control, reversing "no logout endpoint"), D112 (a server-advertised version surface), D126 (there is now an image read path through the app, and `/api` vs `/img` are separated by a Firebase Hosting rewrite, not a load-balancer URL map).*

### D25 — Optimistic concurrency on Firestore `updateTime`; reject-and-reconcile UX *(Linear: OFC-170)*
Concurrency uses Firestore's server-authoritative `updateTime` as the token, surfaced as an `ETag` and required back as `If-Match`; a stale write is rejected with `412`, document-level granularity. `lastModified` remains a separate human-facing "last updated" display field. On a `412` the client repulls, **preserves the user's unsaved edits**, and shows a read-only "these fields changed underneath you" notice for manual reconciliation — no auto-merge.
**Why:** A wall-clock `lastModified` is a weak token (millisecond collisions, client-trust); `updateTime` is strong and monotonic, and Firestore enforces it as a native write precondition — one atomic conditional write, no read-modify-write race. The reconcile-UX is the best-practice middle ground: full auto-merge is over-built for conflicts this rare, but pure discard needlessly destroys the user's work; rejecting at the server guarantees no silent clobber. Field-level OCC (auto-accept disjoint edits) is a possible future refinement.

*Later updated by: D109 (the preserve-unsaved-edits behavior extended from the 412 path to the 401 expired-session path).*

### D26 — Cache coherence via a per-instance Firestore snapshot listener
Each Cloud Run instance hydrates its in-memory `profiles` (and `majors`) cache on cold start, then subscribes to a Firestore snapshot listener so every write propagates to every instance automatically; the writing instance also updates its own cache synchronously for read-your-writes. `users` docs are read on demand, not cached wholesale. Correctness holds at any instance count; max-instances caps remain a cost lever only.
**Why:** Writes are write-through (durability never depends on a clean shutdown). The listener is the clean form of "re-read on change" and removes the need to reason about instance count for correctness — Cloud Run can transiently run two instances during a deploy even with the cap at one, and the listener makes that a non-event. It also future-proofs Book for a larger user base.

*Later updated by: D83 (`max-instances = 1` retires the "correct at any instance count" framing; the snapshot listener is demoted to a deploy-window safety net).*

### D27 — External/third-party API stays deferred; the Linter gets a first-party path (extends D11)
The MVP API is solely the session-authenticated, same-origin backend for the SPA: no API keys, no per-app credentials, no public contract. The PBE News Linter — a trusted first-party, non-browser consumer of name/class-year data — is given a scoped access path (not the public-API mechanism), designed in Session 5.
**Why:** No known third-party use case justifies building an API-auth framework now (per D11). The Linter is a real dependency but is internal and trusted, so it does not need the full external-API surface — just enough scoped access to read the fields it canonicalizes against.

### D28 — Verification is coupled to content edits (refines D19)
Verification status is no longer independent of content. "Verified" is represented solely by the presence of `lastVerifiedDate` (with `verifiedBy`) — no separate flag, no audit history. On a profile `PATCH` that actually changes content: if the editor is the **owner** (their Constitution ID matches the profile, whatever their role), the save auto-(re)verifies it (`lastVerifiedDate = today`, `verifiedBy =` the owner); if the editor is a **manager/admin editing someone else's** profile, a previously verified profile becomes unverified (both fields cleared), while an already-unverified one stays unverified. The explicit Verify action (`POST .../verify`) is retained for the owner's no-change confirmation and for a manager/admin to re-verify after an edit (with the cautioned copy). The unverify is a **deterministic server-side consequence**, not a client request — the manager-facing "you're modifying a user-verified profile; saving will mark it unverified" dialog is courtesy only (Cancel = don't send the PATCH; Save anyway = send it; no API force-flag). **Exception — deceased profiles:** once `isDeceased` is true, edits do not change verification state at all (a verified deceased profile stays verified), since there is no living owner to attest.
**Why:** "Verified" should mean the content reflects what the owner last confirmed; a flag floating free of the data could assert verification over fields the owner never saw. An owner's own edit *is* an act of confirmation; a non-owner's edit diverges the data from the owner's last confirmation and must therefore drop the status until re-confirmed. Server-enforcing the transition keeps the invariant true regardless of client. The deceased exception trusts that the deceased flag — and any accompanying edits — are made deliberately, and avoids an "unverify the dead" workflow. Because `lastVerifiedDate`/`verifiedBy` are now system-managed outputs of these rules, whether the admin CSV bulk-import may set or preserve them is a tension deferred to a later session (open question).

*Later updated by: D68 (the open question resolved — verification fields are import-ignored, a changed row unverifies), D80 (consent/verification snapshotted at mark-deceased and restored on un-mark), N73 (verification read reclassified restricted→public — visible to all brothers, verifier Canonical Name shown; the write coupling here is unchanged — OFC-207/208).*

---

## Session 4a — UI Foundations (2026-06-04)

Deliverables: `PRD.md` §5 (the foundational, cross-page portion) and the §3.2 deferred-list addition; the Directory, Profile, and Admin page specifics are deferred to Sessions 4b and 4c. Resolves the component-library, preference-persistence, theming-mechanism, and URL/History open questions; the visual-design *content* is deferred to a dedicated Claude Design pass.

### D29 — UI built on shadcn/ui (Radix + Tailwind); TanStack Table/Virtual for the data grid
The frontend's component layer is shadcn/ui — accessible Radix UI primitives styled with Tailwind CSS, with component source vendored into the repo rather than imported as a versioned package. The Directory page's data grid is assembled from TanStack Table (headless sorting/filtering/column-ordering) rendered with shadcn table primitives, plus TanStack Virtual for row virtualization. Theming is Tailwind's CSS-variable token layer.
**Why:** React fits a small four-page app and has the deepest AI-codegen training data (continuing D10). shadcn adds best-in-class accessibility from Radix — directly serving the WCAG-AA policy (D32) — and a CSS-variable token layer that satisfies the "styles in one place, easy to re-theme" requirement. The vendored-component model means no heavy runtime styling engine and, critically, no paid tier on the features our hardest screen needs: drag-to-reorder columns and virtualization are free with TanStack, whereas MUI's DataGrid puts column reordering behind a paid Pro license — the paywall landing exactly on the Directory grid. Mantine was the runner-up; MUI ranked third for this reason. Update model: the substrate (Radix, Tailwind) updates through npm normally, so a11y/security fixes flow in automatically; the vendored wrappers are owned and updated selectively via git diff/merge, trading automatic-but-opaque updates for ownership and freedom from surprise breaking changes — a favorable trade for a small, long-lived app.

### D30 — Client-only UI preferences persist in `localStorage`, not cookies (supersedes the original cookie assumption)
Font size, dark-mode preference, and the Directory's column selection/order persist in `localStorage`. The original assumption of cookies is superseded.
**Why:** A cookie is transmitted to the server on every request; these three preferences are purely client concerns the backend never needs, so cookies would add useless per-request overhead. `localStorage` is the purpose-built store for client-only state. The one legitimate reason to keep a theme in a cookie — letting a server render the correct theme on first paint — does not apply, because Book is a pure client-rendered SPA on static hosting (D10): a tiny inline script reads `localStorage` and sets the theme class before React starts, avoiding any flash. Cookies remain correct for the auth session and Mixpanel, which genuinely travel to a server — the principle is "cookies for what the server needs, `localStorage` for what only the browser needs."

### D31 — Navigation, routing, and a three-bucket URL/state model
Routing uses React Router plus nuqs (type-safe URL search-parameter state). Routes: `/` (Directory), `/brother/:id` (Profile), `/brother/:id/edit` (edit mode as its own URL, guarded by an unsaved-changes prompt), `/brother/new` (Add Brother, admin-only), `/admin` (admin-only); role guards are UX only, with real enforcement server-side (D5/D19). Application state is partitioned by contract into three stores: the **view** (search text, filters, sort) in the **URL query string** — shareable, bookmarkable, History-API-updated silently; **transient per-entry context** (the starred-only toggle and scroll position) in **History API state** — restored by the back button but never carried in a shared link; and the **column lens** (column selection/order) in **`localStorage`** (D30) — a personal preference deliberately kept out of the URL. All navigation uses real `<a href>` anchors. Scroll restoration on return to the Directory is a firm requirement.
**Why:** React Router is ubiquitous with the deepest codegen support (consistent with D10); nuqs supplies the typed-search-param ergonomics the Directory needs without adopting a second router ecosystem. Edit-as-its-own-URL lets the back button exit editing and makes "edit my profile" deep-linkable. The three-bucket split follows from what each store *means*: a shared link should reproduce the *view* but not impose the sender's personal column lens, and the starred toggle is kept out of the URL specifically because stars are per-user (D12) — a shared `?starred=1` would show the recipient's stars, not the sender's; History state gives back-button restoration without that confusion, and is the natural home for scroll position too. Real anchors make native shift/ctrl/cmd-click open an independent SPA instance that rebuilds purely from the URL — which is also why the view-state must live in the URL — and is an accessibility win. Scroll restoration is implemented over the virtualized list (saving/restoring the virtualizer's offset), not via native browser scroll memory, which virtualization defeats.

*Later updated by: N79 (a fourth state bucket — per-instance transient row selection, above the three; OFC-196), N80 (the masthead's clean-slate `reset` intent vs. the place-preserving back-navigation; OFC-194).*

### D32 — Accessibility (WCAG 2.1 AA) as policy, and a width-based responsive baseline
Book targets WCAG 2.1 AA as a first-class policy requirement, not a nice-to-have. Beyond the keyboard/focus/ARIA behavior that semantic HTML and Radix supply by construction, Book commits to: a visible (styled, never removed) focus indicator; AA color contrast verified in both light and dark themes (a gate on any palette chosen in the visual-design pass); never encoding meaning in color alone; programmatically-linked, announced form errors; honoring `prefers-reduced-motion`; a skip-to-content link; and alt text on images set to the canonical name. Responsive layout switches on viewport width, not device class: below Tailwind's `md` breakpoint (~768px) the Directory grid collapses to stacked cards; at `md` and above the full grid shows with horizontal scroll as needed. Touch targets are ≥ ~44×44px, and layouts must remain usable at the largest font scale.
**Why:** The editor stated rigorous, complete a11y as a matter of policy for applications of this kind, and the older-alum audience makes it doubly apt. Building on Radix/semantic HTML means most of the AA baseline is structural rather than bolted on. Switching on width rather than "device" is the honest model — there is no reliable tablet signal — and it correctly gives a portrait tablet the grid and a narrowed desktop window the cards. WCAG's reflow rule exempts data tables, so the horizontally-scrolling grid is conformant; the card collapse is a UX choice for small screens, not a compliance one.

---

## Session 4b — The Directory Page (2026-06-04)

Deliverable: `PRD.md` §5.6, grown incrementally as the session proceeds. These four decisions cover the column model, sorting, Name Search, and deceased handling; the structured filter grammar, star interactions, the row-open behavior, the manager/administrator actions, and the virtualization/thumbnail-prefetch tuning remain open in the 4b docket (resolved later this session, D38–D42).

### D33 — Directory grid column model
The grid's four left columns are fixed and non-reorderable — **Select, Star, Thumbnail, Canonical Name** — and **freeze as a contiguous block** during horizontal scroll. The remaining columns are user-selectable and drag-reorderable, defaulting *identically for every role* to **Class Year, Major, Email, Telephone, City, State/Province, Country**; the selection/order is the personal "column lens" in `localStorage` (D30/D31). **Select** is shown only to managers/admins in MVP, gated by a *single capability predicate* (not role checks spread through the grid) so the deferred "Share this selection" feature (PRD §3.2) can later enable it for all brothers in one change. **Major** displays the derived name of the brother's *primary* (first-listed) major, an order the brother controls on their profile — making the profile's major-ordering load-bearing. **Class Year** stays its own column despite also appearing inside Canonical Name. The restricted-class fields managers/admins may see are available to them as *additional selectable columns, off by default*.
**Why:** The fixed left block carries identity plus the per-row controls, so freezing it keeps both anchored while reading a wide table. Defaulting every role to the same clean column set matches how managers/admins use Book like any brother most of the time, with restricted columns added on demand (e.g. "Last Verified" to chase stale records). Gating Select behind one predicate turns the future all-user-selection feature into a one-line enablement rather than a refactor. Showing the brother-ordered primary major gives the brother control over their one-line directory identity. Keeping Class Year as its own column buys numeric sort and range-filtering that a year embedded in a display string cannot provide.

### D34 — Directory sort behavior
Clicking a column header sorts by it ascending; a second click toggles descending. Sorting is **single-column**. Initial load sorts **Canonical Name ascending**. The Canonical Name column sorts by **last name → first name → class year** (not by the `First Last` display string), and that same canonical order is the **universal secondary key** under every primary column. The active sort lives in the URL query string (D31).
**Why:** Last-name-then-first-then-year is the directory order people expect; sorting Canonical Name by its displayed `First Last` string would surprise. One universal secondary key means every column's ties resolve into a readable, predictable name order instead of an arbitrary one. Single-column sort keeps the interaction model simple (one click, one sort); the sort belongs in the URL because it is part of the shareable view (D31).

### D35 — Name Search: fuzzy + phonetic over tokenized name fields
The Directory's search box (labeled **"Name Search"**) matches *names only* — `firstName`, `middleName`, `lastName`, `fullLegalName`, `mugName`, and the canonical name — combining **Fuse.js** typo tolerance with **phonetic** matching via the **talisman** library (starting on **Double Metaphone**, with **Beider-Morse** held in reserve and A/B-tested in Session 6). Name fields are **tokenized** at load on spaces *and* hyphens, with **AND across query tokens**. Phonetic codes are **computed at load, never stored** (an IndexedDB memo is the escape hatch if profiling ever demands one). Search is live/incremental, **AND-composed** with the active filters and the deceased default, lives in the URL (D31), **always honors the user's chosen sort** (no relevance override; no separate "Best match" sort), and highlights matched characters in results.
**Why:** A globally-drawn brotherhood produces names other brothers routinely misspell, so matching must tolerate both typos (Fuse.js) and sound-alikes (phonetic) — they catch different error classes, and the international names make phonetic an MVP need, not a nicety. Tokenizing on spaces and hyphens and indexing middle and mug names is what makes hyphenated names, middle-name-users, and adopted mug names ("Lissajous Figure," "Hilbert Space Pilot") findable; AND across query tokens keeps multi-word queries precise. Keeping the box name-only and labeling it "Name Search" avoids fuzzy noise from non-name fields and sets honest expectations — employer search has little value when only a *current* employer is stored, and widening later is cheap because search is client-side. Computing phonetic codes at load (not storing them) follows the derived-data principle (D15), keeps the algorithm tunable for the Double-Metaphone↔Beider-Morse experiment, and avoids payload bloat that would actually worsen the multi-tab case it might seem to help. Honoring the user's sort respects a setting they deliberately made; small result sets plus match highlighting serve the find-fast need without a second, modal sort mechanism that would break the "sorting is clicking a column header" model.

*Later updated by: D66 (the Double-Metaphone vs. Beider-Morse choice decided by an offline, recall-favoring harness), D110 (the index built in a Web Worker; D35's IndexedDB memo dropped), D123 (a curated common-nickname / diminutives expansion, A/B-tunable, built in the same Web Worker).*

### D36 — Deceased brothers: default-hidden, an Include-deceased toggle, and a dual marker
The grid defaults to living brothers (`deceased.isDeceased = false`); an **"Include deceased"** checkbox beside the Name Search box overrides it, with that choice carried in the shareable view (URL query, D31). Deceased brothers, when shown, carry a **dark diagonal bar across one corner** of the thumbnail/headshot *plus* a textual **"In Memoriam"** marker on the row/card and in the image's accessible name.
**Why:** The directory is overwhelmingly used to reach *living* brothers, so hiding the deceased by default is the right resting state, while an explicit, shareable toggle serves the real cases (looking specifically for a deceased brother). The diagonal-corner-bar is a convention recognizable from genealogy tools; pairing it with the word "In Memoriam" — visible and in the accessible name — satisfies the policy that meaning is never carried by a visual alone (D32).

### D37 — State/Province: a US + Canada controlled vocabulary over free text (amends D18)
`address.stateProvince` becomes a **bundled controlled vocabulary** when `address.country` is `US` or `CA` — the 2-letter USPS state/territory code (including `DC` and the military codes `AA`/`AE`/`AP`) or the 2-letter Canadian province/territory code, stored as the code with the display name derived — and stays **free text** for every other country (or when country is unset). Entry renders a dropdown for US/CA and a text box otherwise (Session 4c); validation requires a valid code when country is US/CA and a value is present. The subdivision lists are bundled build-time constants, not Firestore (like the ISO country list, D18). Amends D18, which had left `stateProvince` free text.
**Why:** State/Province exists to power filter / sort / group ("everyone in Massachusetts"), and free text breaks all three — "MA," "Mass," "Massachusetts," and "Masachussets" never coincide, so they filter separately, sort to four places, and never group. The membership is overwhelmingly North American (the MITAA dataset shows 835 living brothers in US+CA versus 37 elsewhere — international students overwhelmingly settle in the US after graduating), so coding just those two countries makes the field work for essentially the whole directory at low cost: the lists are tiny, fixed, universally known, and the MITAA seed data is very likely already in USPS codes (the dropdown prevents new variants and a one-time import pass normalizes legacy rows). Global ISO 3166-2 subdivisions are a large, messy, low-value domain for this dataset, so they stay free text. This extends the D18 principle — code the stable external standard, derive the name — down to the subdivision level only where the standard is small and stable.

### D38 — Directory filtering: a top filter panel, typed controls, and "filterable ⟺ visible"
Structured filters live in a collapsible **filter panel above the grid** (column headers stay reserved for sorting, D34). The governing rule: **a field is filterable by a role exactly when that role can see it as a column** (inherits the visibility projection — D16/D19/D33). Controls are typed: **numeric grammar** text (comma lists + dash ranges, always-4-digit years) for Class Year and Constitution ID; **multi-select** for Major / Country / State-Province (options drawn from observed values); **substring** for City; **boolean/presence** (manager/admin only) for contact completeness, the consent flags, and verification status. Employer/Job Title are excluded from MVP filtering. Filters compose **OR within a field, AND across fields**, with the deceased default (D36) AND-ed in. A **Reset** clears search + filters + sort but not the column lens; all filters live in the URL view state (D31).
**Why:** A top panel groups heterogeneous controls (a year-grammar box, multi-selects, substring boxes) more legibly than per-header popovers and keeps the active filters visible (answering "why am I seeing only 12 rows?"), while leaving header-click solely for sorting. "Filterable ⟺ visible" ties filtering to the projection that already governs visibility, so it adds no new exposure surface and needs no second access path — and it is free because filtering runs client-side over the already-projected data. Typed controls fit each field's nature: a multi-select is the friendly form of an OR comma-list, the grammar text serves ranges, and OR-within / AND-across is the conventional, least-surprising composition the filter grammar implies. The manager/admin presence-and-staleness filters are the practical tools for the directory-maintenance role (D19). Reset preserving the column lens matches the state model: search/filter/sort are transient view state, the column lens is a durable personal preference (D30/D31).

*Later updated by: C3 (composite §12 — `allowCommentReplyEmail` made a consistent manager column and filter).*

### D39 — Stars: a universal pinned column, optimistic toggle, and a deceased-bypassing "Starred only"
The **Star** column is one of the always-present pinned columns, shown to *every* role (unlike Select). Clicking it **optimistically** toggles the brother in the user's private `users.stars` list (`PUT`/`DELETE /api/me/stars/{id}`, D24), reverting on a failed write, and stops propagation so it never also opens the profile. State is shown by shape + fill (filled gold star vs. hollow outline), not color alone (D32). A **"Starred only"** toggle beside the Name Search box lives in **History-API state, not the URL** (D31), and **bypasses the deceased default** — it shows all of the user's starred brothers, living or deceased (the deceased still marked).
**Why:** Stars are everyone's feature and per-user private (D12), so the column is universal while its filter must never travel in a shared link (D31). Optimistic toggling fits a low-stakes, instantly-reversible action at this scale. Bypassing the deceased default matches the intent of a hand-curated list — dropping a starred brother because he has since died would be a surprising "where did he go?" — and is a small, defensible exception to the otherwise-AND filter composition (D38).

*Later updated by: R17 (composite §12 — `arrayUnion`/`arrayRemove` specified for the stars PUT/DELETE), D98 (stars referencing a deleted brother scrubbed on delete), N80 (the masthead clean-slate reset also clears the Starred-only flag; OFC-194), N99 (the Star is also on the Profile page; the set is hoisted to a shell-level provider shared by the Directory and the Profile — OFC-256).*

### D40 — Opening a profile: full-page anchor navigation; modal quick-view deferred *(Linear: OFC-167)*
Clicking a Directory row navigates to the full **Profile** page at `/brother/:id` via a real anchor (plain click in place, modified-click in a new tab — D31); the whole row is the target **except** the interactive Star and Select cells. A **modal quick-view is deferred** (not MVP). The per-row **"Search News"** link stays out (Ghost content search deferred, D11).
**Why:** The full page is required regardless (it hosts editing), so a modal would only ever be an additive second view; it would also undercut the anchor/URL/new-tab model (D31) and need its own URL representation to preserve back-button and deep-linking behavior, while the "don't lose my place" benefit it promises is already delivered by D31's scroll-and-filter restoration on Back — and a full-screen modal is weakest on phone-width cards anyway. Real anchors give new-tab opening and keyboard activation for free. Deferring is cheap: a quick-view can be added post-MVP with no data or architecture change.

### D41 — Directory manager/administrator action bar and selection model
A manager/admin-only **action bar** above the grid (gated by the same capability predicate as the Select column, D33) carries **Export** (manager + admin) and **Add Brother / Delete / Regenerate Thumbnails** (admin only). Row selection is per-row checkboxes plus a header **select-all scoped to the current filtered view** (over the whole match set, not just the virtualized window); selection is transient and **clears on any search/filter change**. **Export is client-side** from the in-memory, already-projected dataset — the selected rows, or the whole current view if none — as CSV (SCHEMA §10), never images. Delete and Regenerate Thumbnails run behind confirmations with result summaries; Add Brother routes to `/brother/new`.
**Why:** Gating the bar and the Select column together keeps the entire bulk-maintenance surface out of the ordinary brother's view. Clearing the selection on a view change scopes every destructive action to what is currently visible, eliminating the "deleted rows I couldn't see" hazard at the small cost of occasional re-selection. Client-side export falls straight out of the bulk-download architecture (D4) and inherits the per-role projection (D5) for free, so no export endpoint or second projection path is needed. Strong confirmations with result summaries are the standing treatment of destructive and bulk operations.

*Later updated by: D90 (a dedicated consent-aware MITAA export), D92 (a thin export-audit ping; generation stays client-side), D100 (Directory bulk-delete dropped; single-record delete kept), N79 (selection now persists across the view — clear-on-view-change reversed; select-all unions the visible view; OFC-196).*

### D42 — Directory virtualization and thumbnail prefetch policy
The Directory grid is a **single virtualized continuous list of the entire current result set** (TanStack Virtual, D29) — no pagination, no Load-More — with a result-count readout, a sticky header row on vertical scroll, and virtualizer scroll-offset restoration on Back (D31); the sub-`md` card layout is virtualized the same way. Thumbnails (D9: lazy + prefetch, CDN-served, out of the payload) use **overscan-tied lookahead loading** (≈1–2 viewport-heights, build-tuned) plus a **bounded idle-time prefetch of the current result set in scroll order** (`requestIdleCallback`, cancelled on view change); the 96×96 box is reserved to prevent layout shift, with a generic avatar for headshot-less brothers.
**Why:** With the full dataset in memory (D4) there is nothing to paginate, so a virtualized single list gives instant scrolling with a light DOM and no Load-More friction — this also settles the "paginate vs. infinite-scroll" question left open earlier. The prefetch policy concretizes the knob D9 deferred: lookahead keeps near rows ready, idle prefetch makes deeper scrolling seamless, and both are cheap because thumbnails are a few-KB immutable, cacheable objects (D17/D23) — single-digit megabytes even for the whole set — while keeping them out of the initial payload preserves the fast text-only bulk load (D9). Reserving the image box avoids cumulative layout shift, an accessibility and UX win (D32).

*Later updated by: U6 (composite §12 — `aria-rowcount`/`-rowindex`/`-setsize` required and tested on the virtualized grid), D126 (thumbnails are served by the app from a private bucket, not the CDN edge; the lookahead/idle-prefetch policy is unchanged).*

---

## Session 4c — The Profile Page (2026-06-04)

Deliverable: `PRD.md` §5.7, grown end to end. These decisions cover the view/edit model, the per-role rendering, the privacy/consent toggles, the special field controls, the headshot crop flow, verification surfacing, the In Memoriam treatment, validation/saving/conflict handling, and the administrator on-profile controls. The Admin page (§5.8) remains open in the 4c docket (resolved later this session, D52).

### D43 — Profile page: view/edit model and a responsive, reading-order-preserving two-up layout
The Profile page has a view mode and an edit mode, edit mode being its own URL (`/brother/:id/edit`) with an unsaved-changes guard (D31); an Edit button (owner / manager / admin) enters it, Save / Cancel leave it, and Cancel discards every pending change including a staged photo. The layout is a **responsive multi-section grid** that places sections **two-up at `md`+** and collapses to a single column below `md` (the phone form). The firm constraint: **visual placement never diverges from source / reading / tab order** (WCAG 1.3.2 / 2.4.3), so only sections already adjacent in the reading order are paired side by side. Structural default: a full-width **identity header** (headshot, Canonical Name, class year, Constitution ID, and the name fields — "Names" folds into identity) over three paired rows — Contact ‖ Emergency, Professional&personal ‖ Relationships, Preferences&consent ‖ Record-status. Exact composition is the Claude Design pass's job.
**Why:** A 2-D grid is more attractive and uses wide-screen real estate, while the single-column collapse gives the mobile form for free. Pairing only adjacent sections is what keeps the two-column visual from scrambling the screen-reader/keyboard order — the accessibility-safe way to get a 2-D layout. Folding Names into the header matches how the page reads (the names *are* the identity) and avoids a thin standalone section.

### D44 — Permission-conditioned rendering: one layout, four role projections
The page renders from the same per-role visibility projection as the bulk download (D5/D16/D19), so a field the caller may not see is **absent, not merely hidden**. Owner: full record, all fields editable except the system-managed ones and the immutable ID. Peer: public + shared-toggle contact fields; the entire restricted block is absent (neither value nor existence shown); no Edit. Manager: peer's view **plus** the restricted block rendered **read-only / visibly locked**, with a **"this field is private" marker** standing in for any value behind an off-toggle — seeing *that* a field is hidden without seeing the value; may edit visible directory fields and set deceased, but not change consent/privacy or see through an off-toggle. Admin: everything, the only role that edits another brother's privacy/consent, and the carrier of the on-profile admin controls (D51).
**Why:** The page is the visible face of the server-side projection — the projection decides what data arrives, this decides how it is presented (editable / locked / marked-private) per role. The "private" marker for managers operationalizes the housekeeper model (D19): a manager can coach a brother and chase stale records by seeing *that* something is private, without the value, which only the owner and admin (the override role) ever see.

### D45 — Privacy & consent as two-position switches that state each side's consequence, defaulting open
All six boolean fields — the four share toggles (`shareEmail` / `sharePhone` / `shareAddress` / `shareEmergency`) and the two consent flags (`allowNewsletterEmail` / `allowShareWithMITAA`) — render as a **two-position switch that states the concrete consequence of each side in plain language** (e.g. "Brothers can reach you by email" ↔ "Brothers cannot reach you by email"), defaulting to the open / opted-in position. Each share-toggle sits **inline with the field group it governs**; the two consent switches sit in the Preferences & consent section. Owner/admin may change them; managers see them locked. The treatment is **presentation only** — the stored booleans and their `true` defaults are unchanged.
**Why:** A deliberate, transparent nudge (Thaler & Sunstein): the pro-sharing default does most of the work and stating both consequences makes any opt-out an informed, considered act rather than an inattentive click. It is ethical here because the audience is a closed, consensual, members-only directory whose purpose is mutual reachability, the data never leaves the brotherhood, and the consequence copy keeps it honest (not a dark pattern). It directly cures the MITAA failure mode — a single ambiguous global opt-out ("no emails") that silently swept PBE in — by naming exactly what each toggle controls. Stating both consequences as text also satisfies meaning-not-by-color (D32).

*Later updated by: D59 (`allowShareWithMITAA` redefined as a master switch over the contact set), D89 (MITAA sharing defaults to opt-in; switch copy clarified), D93 (`shareEmergency` defaults off; `shareSpousePartner` toggle added), D113 (copy: active-side consequence inline, counterfactual moved to the help tip).*

### D46 — Special field controls: chips, conditional state/province, big-brother picker, progressive-disclosure repeatables
Majors are edited as **draggable colored chips** styled after Material 3 but **built in the shadcn/Tailwind stack** (M3 is a visual reference; importing MUI is rejected, consistent with D29), **colored by base course number** (color a grouping aid; code text carries meaning; full course name in the accessible name; palette AA-gated in the design pass), added from a searchable `majors` combobox (retired codes de-emphasized; no duplicates), reorderable by drag **and** by keyboard Move / Make-first controls; the UI says "the major listed first is what appears in the directory listing" and **never says "primary."** State/Province is a control that **watches `country`** — US/CA dropdown vs. free text (D37) — defaulting `country` to `US` on new/blank addresses, clearing an invalid subdivision on country change. Big Brother is a **typeahead picker by canonical name** (excludes self; server-authoritative cycle check) with the **derived Little Brothers** shown read-only. Links (≤5) and emergency contacts (≤2) use **progressive disclosure** — an "Add another" affordance reveals the next empty row, no wall of blank fields.
**Why:** Chips are visually modern and the one place in Book where they earn their keep; building them in-stack keeps the single component system (D29) rather than reintroducing MUI's weight/paywall. Dropping "primary" matches how people speak (a true double major has no primary) and says plainly what the order *does*. Defaulting country to US fits a ≈96% US/CA membership while keeping every other country one click away. Progressive disclosure avoids a daunting field wall and scales identically for both repeatables. Keyboard reorder controls keep drag accessible (D32).

### D47 — Headshot crop-on-upload, staged until Save
Changing a photo opens a **react-easy-crop** modal (in a Radix Dialog) with a fixed **1:1 frame**, zoom, and pan; on Apply the client sends the cropped region and the **backend** normalizes it to the canonical **512×512 WEBP headshot** and derives the **96×96 thumbnail** (DATABASE-SCHEMA §7). The cropped image is **staged with the form and committed only on Save** (Cancel discards it); "Remove photo" stages the same way. The crop modal must be keyboard-operable (zoom buttons, arrow-key pan, focus trap) to meet AA (D32).
**Why:** Client-owns-the-crop / server-owns-the-encoding keeps human judgment where it belongs (the group-photo case) and the canonical format/dimensions/thumbnail in one authoritative place — which is also what makes admin "Regenerate Thumbnails" possible. Staging until Save (over immediate upload-on-crop) honors the page's abandonable edit-session contract (D31): Cancel must mean Cancel, including the photo. Because the headshot is its own sub-resource (D24), the two-call Save is sequenced text-then-photo (D50).

*Later updated by: D98 (GCS objects written first, the `headshotVersion` pointer advanced last), D107 (decoded-dimension cap + magic-byte check on upload).*

### D48 — Verification surfacing and the owner staleness nudge
The restricted Record-status section shows "Last verified YYYY-MM-DD by [name]" (linked verifier = provenance) or "Not verified," with a **role-specific Verify button** (owner: "Confirm my information is correct"; manager/admin: the cautioned "verify only after confirming directly with this brother," §4.3). The D28 coupling surfaces as: owner edit → server auto-(re)verifies with a quiet toast; manager/admin editing a currently-verified profile → a **courtesy "saving will mark it unverified" dialog** (Cancel / Save anyway; the unverify is the server's deterministic consequence, not a client flag). When the **owner views their own profile last verified > 2 years ago, or never**, a gentle banner invites confirmation. On a **deceased** profile the Verify button is hidden and verification is frozen.
**Why:** Periodic re-verification is the directory's chief freshness mechanism, so the owner needs a frictionless "still correct" affordance and a timely nudge; two years is a reasonable staleness horizon. The courtesy dialog warns without overriding the server-enforced invariant. Freezing on deceased is right because the obituary data is staff-attested when set but nothing else can be owner-attested any longer — the page-level status rightly stops moving (the editor's own observation, captured here).

*Later updated by: D80 (prior consent/verification state snapshotted at mark-deceased and restored on un-mark).*

### D49 — In Memoriam treatment and a soft confirmation to mark deceased
A deceased profile opens with a **large, respectful "In Memoriam" banner**, the headshot carries the Directory's diagonal corner bar (D36), and the public deceased fields (date of death, obituary link, PBE News In Memoriam article link) display below. Setting deceased is manager/admin-only and sits behind a **gentle, deliberate confirmation** ("Mark [name] as deceased? This applies the In Memoriam treatment and removes them from the default directory view") — distinct in tone from the destructive Delete modal; it reveals the deceased fields and triggers the verification freeze (D48).
**Why:** The intent is a dignified memorial, not a status flag; the deceased fields are public-class so the whole brotherhood sees the memorial. The soft confirmation matches the action's gravity and social sensitivity without the alarm of a destructive-delete dialog, since marking deceased is significant but reversible.

*Later updated by: D80 (the snapshot/restore that makes the reversible mark-deceased fully reversible, including the email-consent flags), D122 (optional birth/death-year fields + a b./d. lifespan display; the confirmation stays pure and reveals-and-focuses the now-five deceased fields).*

### D50 — Validation, saving, and conflict handling
Validation is **inline / per-field**, programmatically associated and announced (D32), firing on blur and re-validated in full on Save (focus moves to the first error); rules are exactly DATABASE-SCHEMA §8 (including the **alternate-email field disabled until a primary email exists** and the **Constitution ID read-only** except on the Add-Brother flow). Client and server **share one validation module** (TS end-to-end, D3) so they cannot drift. Save is one user action over a possibly two-call sequence ordered **courtesy-unverify-dialog → text `PATCH` with `If-Match` → (412 → reconcile) → staged photo `PUT`/`DELETE` → done**. A **412** preserves the user's unsaved edits, re-fetches, and shows an announced **"This profile was changed while you were editing"** notice (deliberately *not* "by someone else," since per-tab SPA instances mean it may be the same user, D31) highlighting the changed fields for manual reconciliation — no auto-merge (D25).
**Why:** On-blur + on-save is the accessible, low-nag validation pattern; a shared module is the single-source principle applied to validation. Ordering the `PATCH` (the real `If-Match` concurrency guard, D25) before the photo `PUT` means a conflict stops the Save before the photo is touched, leaving the staged crop intact. The neutral conflict copy is the honest wording once you accept that independent per-tab instances make "the other editor" possibly yourself.

*Later updated by: D109 (the 401-mid-Save path gets the same non-destructive recovery as the 412 flow, resuming with the original `If-Match`).*

### D51 — Administrator on-profile controls: Delete and an explicit role-selector Toggle Privileges
The individual brother's profile carries the two admin-only controls (resolving their home as the profile, not a central screen). **Delete Brother** sits behind a scary confirmation in the same family as the Directory bulk delete (D41), calls `DELETE /api/profiles/{id}` (D24), and returns to the Directory. **Toggle Privileges** uses an **explicit role selector** (Brother / Manager / Administrator) behind a confirmation (replacing an earlier cycle-through-roles idea), and **prevents demoting the last remaining administrator** (warning on self-demotion) to avoid an admin lockout.
**Why:** Acting on the brother you are looking at is the natural locus for both controls, and the Directory's bulk Delete (D41) already covers the multi-brother case, so the two delete paths coexist by intent. An explicit selector is clearer and less error-prone than cycling for a consequential change, and the last-admin safeguard is cheap insurance against locking a 1–2-admin organization out of its own admin functions.

*Later updated by: C13 (composite §12 — "Toggle Privileges" renamed "Change role"), D105 (no step-up on the role-grant / single-delete surface), D106 (the last-admin invariant enforced server-side with before/after role audit).*

### D52 — Admin page: a whole-database control panel of UI surfaces (logic deferred)
The Admin page (`/admin`, admin-only, server-enforced) hosts Book's **whole-database** operations, complementing the Profile page's per-brother controls (D51) and the Directory's selection-scoped bulk actions (D41). Four groups, specified in 4c as **surfaces, confirmations, and result reporting** with the mechanics deferred: (1) **Backup & restore** — a Download-backup button and a Restore-from-backup upload gated by a **typed acknowledgment** (the most destructive action in Book — it replaces the entire database) plus a prompt to back up first; the backup *format* is Session 6. (2) **Bulk-CSV import** in the canonical format (SCHEMA §10) with a **dry-run preview** ("will update X, add Y") before an explicit Apply, then a result summary; format edge cases and the D28 verification-field tension are Session 6. (3) **Ghost sync** — a Sync-with-Ghost trigger and a discrepancy-report shell; the sync and its conflict policy are Session 5. (4) **Regenerate all thumbnails** — a whole-database rebuild from stored headshots (skipping headshot-less profiles), behind a confirmation with a summary. The per-brother **role toggle is not here** — it lives on the Profile page (D51); majors-vocabulary curation is a noted future home (Session 6).
**Why:** The Admin page's natural identity is whole-database maintenance, which is why backup/restore, bulk import, Ghost sync, and a global thumbnail rebuild all belong together here, while scoped actions live where the scope is chosen — a selection on the Directory, an individual on his Profile. The dry-run preview and typed-acknowledgment restore match the standing preference for non-destructive verification before mass or irreversible operations. The global Regenerate-all specifically removes a footgun in approximating it from the Directory, where the deceased-hidden default would silently skip deceased brothers' thumbnails. Specifying surfaces now while deferring logic keeps 4c focused on UI, leaving the data-touching mechanics to the integration (Session 5) and operations (Session 6) sessions that own them.

*Later updated by: D55 (Ghost-sync mechanics), D63 (backup/restore semantics), D68 (bulk-import rules), D100 (no online bulk-write path — import deferred, restore offline, Directory bulk-delete dropped).*

### D53 — In-context help: a layered, embedded help model with toggle-tip disclosures (4c addendum) *(Linear: OFC-173)*
Book embeds usage guidance in the pages rather than relying on the user manual, on the principle that good UX should not require a manual — doubly apt for the 60+ audience. Help is provided **where a control's purpose or usage is not self-evident** (not on every element — blanket help icons clutter, and clutter itself impedes older users), via a **layered model**: (1) a persistent visible label on every field; (2) persistent, `aria-describedby`-linked helper text for anything needed during use (e.g., that the class-year field accepts ranges/lists, `1980-1989, 1992`); (3) a supplementary native `placeholder` as a light, AA-contrast example that auto-clears on input and never carries essential instructions or serves as the label (and whose text is accurate to the field — the name-only Directory search, D35, not "names, years, majors"); (4) a **toggle-tip** "?" control for deeper explanation; and, optionally, (5) a future global "Show help" toggle. The toggle tip is a **question-mark-in-a-circle SVG (Lucide `CircleHelp`, themed via `currentColor`, sized in `rem`)** inside a real `<button>` (accessible name "Help: …", ≥44px target, SVG `aria-hidden`), built on **Radix Popover** — click to open, click/Escape/outside-click to close with focus return, the text announced to assistive tech on open. The in-page help content and the `USER-MANUAL` deliverable **share a single source** so they cannot drift. Lives in `PRD.md` §5.9 (a cross-cutting foundational standard, applied across §5.6–5.8).
**Why:** Hover tooltips fail exactly our users — they demand precise sustained hover, vanish on drift/timeout, and don't exist on touch (tablets/phones are common in this cohort); a click-toggle disclosure is operable by fine-motor, touch, and keyboard users alike. Placeholder text is an accessibility anti-pattern when misused, so it stays supplementary (label + helper text carry the load) and AA-contrast-checked, and it must vanish on input (native `placeholder`, not a JS value hack). Help-where-non-obvious keeps the resting UI calm — itself an accessibility benefit for older users — while still covering genuinely unguessable affordances like numeric-range entry. An SVG icon (not a Unicode overlay or emoji) is the only form whose color and size the theme CSS fully controls. Reusing one source for both the embedded tips and the manual keeps documentation accurate and consistent with the design decisions. This was a late 4c addendum, raised after the page work but foundational and cross-cutting.

*Later updated by: D111 (help split along the WCAG line — AA-baseline labels/instructions ship per page), D113 (reuses the toggle-tip for the privacy-copy counterfactual).*

---

## Session 5 — Integrations & Data Sync (2026-06-05)

Deliverables: `ENGINEERING-DESIGN.md` §5 (the four integration surfaces), `PRD.md` §6 (integration requirements) plus the §1 composite-system framing, §3.2 deferred-list additions, §3.3 update, §4 capability/visibility additions, and §5.7 Profile-page additions; and amendments to `DATABASE-SCHEMA.md` (three new fields, two new visibility classes, the MITAA master-switch clarification). New artifact: `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS.md`. Resolves all four Session-5 open questions.

### D54 — Book and Ghost are a single composite system
Book is not a standalone application; it is one half of a composite system with Ghost, bound by (at least) three ties: Ghost provides Book's authentication, Book provides Ghost's member management, and a deferred third tie — Ghost content search — would bind them further. The dependency is asymmetric: **Book hard-depends on Ghost** (no Ghost, no sign-in, Book unusable), while **Ghost soft-depends on Book** (member management is routed through Book by design, but Ghost's own admin panel remains a break-glass path). Stated up front in `PRD.md` §1 and `ENGINEERING-DESIGN.md` §5.
**Why:** The composite-system truth has operational teeth — it explains *why* member editing is removed from Ghost and redirected to Book (it's the design, not a hack), sets the disaster-recovery expectation (lose Book and an admin still manages members in Ghost), and warns maintainers that neither system is meant to run alone. Naming the asymmetry is the precise version: only Book is wholly dependent.

*Later updated by: D105 (the Ghost single-point-of-compromise blast radius accepted and documented in a threat model; no step-up added).*

### D55 — Single-master Ghost sync: Book authoritative, Ghost editing disabled and redirected
Book is the system of record for member data; Ghost is downstream. Member-data editing in Ghost is **disabled and its account UI redirected to Book** (override the theme's `data-portal="account"` / `#/portal/account` target, with Ghost Pro JS Code Injection as the fallback). Book **pushes to Ghost in real time on save**: primary `email`, `name` (the Canonical Name, byline form, `(#5247)` disambiguation omitted), `allowNewsletterEmail`, and `allowCommentReplyEmail` — nothing else. The member **lifecycle**: create → `POST` to Ghost, capture `ghostMemberId`, suppress Ghost's signup email; update → `PUT` addressed by `ghostMemberId`; delete → delete the Ghost member; deceased → force both email-consent flags `false` and push the unsubscribes. Sign-in matches on **primary email only** (`alternateEmail` is never matched and never pushed). The admin **"Sync with Ghost" is a read-only reconciliation audit** keyed on `ghostMemberId` (categories: unmatched Ghost members / field drift / missing Ghost member) that **never writes Book from Ghost**. The symmetric "Book's ID inside Ghost" idea (`canonicalName2`) was **rejected as redundant** to `ghostMemberId`.
**Why:** A single master dissolves the two-master conflict at its root and — because the audit never writes Book — permanently removes the failed-push-undo hazard (a Book edit whose Ghost push failed must never be read back as a "change" that reverts it). Real-time push is required because email is the auth join key: Ghost must learn an email change before the brother's next sign-in. Addressing updates by the stored `ghostMemberId` (not by email) is what makes an *email change* unambiguous, since the member is never looked up by the field being changed. `ghostMemberId` alone gives the audit a stable two-way join, so embedding Book's key in Ghost adds nothing — the only thing that would revive it (byline → Book deep-linking) is itself deferred.

*Later updated by: D96 (synchronous diff-based push, create-Ghost-first, email-commit-gated-on-Ghost), D98 (the reconciliation audit widened to Book-internal orphans), D99 (the audit moved to a scheduled job), D101 (immediate admin-reviewed post-restore reconciliation), D103 (a scoped bidirectional exception for the newsletter flag), N65 (Ghost-first-gated update), N68 (`allowCommentReplyEmail` removed — the pushed set drops to email/name/newsletter; deceased forces only the newsletter flag off).*

### D56 — New Ghost-coupled fields and the staff-internal / system visibility classes
*Later updated by: N68 (2026-07-08 — `allowCommentReplyEmail` is removed from Book entirely; it cannot be set via the Ghost Admin API, so the control would be non-functional — OFC-215/OFC-216).*

Three fields are added to `Profile`: **`allowCommentReplyEmail`** (boolean, default `true`, restricted class, pushed to Ghost — required so Book can fully replace Ghost's redirected email-preferences modal); **`adminNote`** (free text, a new **staff-internal** class: manager+admin read/write, invisible to the owner and to peers); and **`ghostMemberId`** (Ghost member UUID, a new **system/internal** class: backend-only, never sent to any client, excluded from the CSV). The visibility taxonomy grows from four classes to six.
**Why:** Redirecting Ghost's Account UI to Book means Book must own every control those Ghost modals held, including the comment-reply preference — otherwise brothers lose a setting they have today. `adminNote` ports the existing Ghost "notes" practice (e.g. "manually added by an admin, 2026-05-10," duplicate-merge history) whose whole value is candid cross-staff coordination — which depends on the brother *not* seeing it, hence a class the owner is excluded from (a first for Book). `ghostMemberId` makes Book→Ghost pushes address the member by a stable handle (see D55) and is pure plumbing, so it belongs in no client projection.

*Later updated by: D70 (the stored Ghost id corrected — Admin-API member id, not uuid), D81 (`ghostMemberUuid` removed from MVP), D106 (`adminNote`/`ghostMemberId` made unwritable via PATCH).*

### D57 — Initial data migration is external one-time tooling, not Book
The pre-launch data load is built as **standalone utilities**, not Book features: a **one-time pull-and-seed utility** (reads from Ghost the member ID, the notes → `adminNote`, and the current newsletter/comment-notification subscription state → `allowNewsletterEmail`/`allowCommentReplyEmail`), and a **special bulk-loader** that writes the merged Constitution/MITAA/Ghost/family-tree/yearbook dataset including `ghostMemberId`. In steady state Book reads Ghost only for authentication and the read-only audit; this is the only time Book data is written *from* Ghost. The growing tool inventory lives in `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS.md`. The earlier multiple-Ghost-accounts-per-brother cleanup is a prerequisite of this migration (the mystery-email project), not Book runtime logic.
**Why:** The load runs exactly once and is a messy manual merge across five sources — no reason to clutter Book with code never used again, and the loader needs fields (`ghostMemberId`) the ordinary CSV import deliberately excludes. Crucially, seeding the email preferences *from* Ghost honors existing opt-outs: starting from the `true` defaults and pushing would silently re-subscribe brothers who had unsubscribed — a real consent violation.

### D58 — Linter access: a read-only roster endpoint authenticated by a Google service-account token
The Linter reads a **read-only roster** — `GET /api/roster` returning `id`, the name fields, `classYear`, `isDeceased`, and the **server-constructed `canonicalName`** (no contact or restricted data) — pulling the whole set once per run and matching locally, never writing back. It authenticates with a **GCP service-account OIDC identity token** (verified at the Cloud Run front door via `roles/run.invoker`, or in-code against Google's JWKS), entirely off the Ghost session path. **Book serves the finished canonical name** rather than sharing a function, refining D15. The scheme is runtime-agnostic.
**Why:** This satisfies D27 (a scoped first-party path, not the public API and not the Ghost bridge) and avoids a static API key that could leak into source control — on GCP the metadata server mints tokens keylessly, and a locally-run Linter uses `gcloud` impersonation.
