Serving the canonical name resolves D15's "one shared function" assumption, which only holds within a single language: the Linter is likely Python (local-LLM tooling) while Book is TypeScript, so one *served output* keeps a single source of truth across the language boundary. Bulk-pull-and-match-locally mirrors Book's own architecture (D4) and fits a tool processing 20–30 articles per issue. Runtime-independence means Book's obligation is fixed before the Linter's own deployment is decided.

*Later updated by: D78 (roster auth: subject-pinned in-code Google-JWKS; the IAM front door dropped as infeasible), D112 (`/api/roster` gains a contract version + deprecation note).*

### D59 — MITAA exchange: manual, low-trust, both directions reuse existing tools
The MITAA exchange stays **manual, occasional, and low-trust** (automated exchange deferred, D11); MITAA is a *suggestion source*, never authoritative, and Book remains the system of record. **Import**'s lead value is catching deaths (often PBE's only notification for older brothers); matching to Constitution IDs and curation happen *outside* Book, and the vetted Book-format CSV goes through the ordinary admin **dry-run bulk import**. **Export** is gated by **`allowShareWithMITAA`**, redefined as a **master switch** over the brother's own contact set (email/phone/postal) that overrides the brother-facing share-toggles (a different audience); emergency-contact data is never shared; **name, class year, and public deceased status/obituary always flow regardless** of the flag. Because the export can require contact values hidden from brothers, **MITAA export is administrator-only**, and is a full snapshot of consenting brothers (changes-only deferred). The format is kept flexible (external mapping script).
**Why:** Everything reuses tools earlier sessions already built (dry-run import D52, consent-filterable directory D38, role-projected export D41), so no MITAA-specific feature is needed. The master-switch definition dissolves the share-toggle-vs-MITAA orthogonality cleanly — each toggle means exactly what it says, scoped to its audience — and the admin-only consequence is desirable anyway. Sending identity + public death data even for opted-out brothers preserves the bidirectional mortality reconciliation (PBE sometimes knows of a death before MITAA) while disclosing nothing MITAA lacks or that isn't public. Flexible format is warranted because MITAA's import requirements are unknown and their export layout may not be fixed.

*Later updated by: D89 (MITAA sharing defaults to opt-in; copy clarified), D90 (a dedicated two-tier consent-aware MITAA export), D100 (MITAA bulk-CSV import deferred to the post-MVP backlog).*

### D60 — Mailman stays out of MVP; replacement directions recorded
Direct integration with PBE's two **MIT-*hosted*** **Mailman 2.1.34** lists — **pbe-brothers-official** (heavily moderated) and **pbe-connect** (unmoderated; any brother broadcasts to all subscribers) — is **deferred** (extends D11). The barrier is control of the *software* (Mailman 2 has no usable API; the instance can't be modified), not of the lists (PBE manages those). If ever built, the likely mechanism is **lightweight HTTP form POSTs with HTML-response parsing** (not browser automation); in-Book vs. side-tool is left open. Two **replacement** directions are logged without choosing: (a) migrate **pbe-brothers-official into Ghost** as a second newsletter (fits a moderated broadcast; would not cover pbe-connect), or (b) **self-host PBE-owned Mailman 3** with a direct Book↔Mailman REST integration (could replace both). Direction (a) implies **multiple-newsletter support** — generalizing the single `allowNewsletterEmail` boolean into a per-newsletter subscription set over a "newsletters" vocabulary, mirroring Ghost's native model — itself deferred.
**Why:** Nothing in the feasibility revisit changes the D11 verdict: no reachable/usable API and a server we can't modify leave only brittle form-scraping with admin-credential handling. The lists are separate plumbing from PBE News (Ghost), so this is correctly decoupled from the newsletter consent already modeled. Recording the replacement paths — especially retiring Mailman *into* Ghost, which dissolves the sync and UI problems rather than perpetuating them — preserves the strategic options without committing MVP effort; the multi-newsletter generalization is the schema consequence to remember if (a) is ever taken up.

---

## Session 6a — Operations & Testing (2026-06-06)

Deliverables: `ENGINEERING-DESIGN.md` §6 (the full Operations section — logging, analytics, backups, TLS, data-maintenance operations, and the testing strategy), plus amendments to `DATABASE-SCHEMA.md` (the `ghostMemberId`/`ghostMemberUuid` split, the import/verification rule, the two carried open items resolved), `ENGINEERING-DESIGN.md` §5.1 (Ghost member-id addressing correction), and `PRD.md` §5.8.2 (dry-run "unverify" count). Session 6 was split into **6a** (operations, testing, last deferred decisions — this session) and **6b** (build/launch plan, USER-MANUAL, close-out). A cross-cutting cleanup this session: all references to the original source notes were removed from the delivered repo docs so the design suite stands alone (the planning-vault process notes kept theirs until the Session-6c close-out, when they too were scrubbed).

### D61 — Logging: structured Cloud Logging with a privacy-disciplined audit stream
Book logs as **structured JSON to `stdout`/`stderr`** (captured by Cloud Logging — no SDK), in three streams: HTTP access logs (from the platform), severity-tagged **diagnostic** logs, and a durable **audit** stream of mutations and security events (sign-in success/denial, JWKS failures, profile CRUD, role changes, deceased-marking, import, backup/restore, Ghost pushes). The audit discipline: log actor id, target id, action, outcome, and changed-field **names** — **never field values**. The audit stream routes to a longer-retention log bucket; operators and a planned **log-reader agent** query it via the **same off-Ghost service-account pattern as the Linter** (`roles/logging.viewer`, D58), and security events drive log-based metrics + alerting.
**Why:** Cloud Run captures the standard streams for free, so structured JSON needs no dependency and fits the "progress to stdout, errors to stderr" convention. Logging field *names* but not *values* keeps protected data from leaking around the server-side privacy projection (§1.4) into logs with different access controls. Structured fields make the agent's queries precise; reusing the Linter's keyless service-account pattern adds no new auth surface.

*Later updated by: P10 (composite §12 — names-not-values extended to all log streams), D91 (the log-reader agent constrained to first-party/local), D92 ("export" added to the audit event list), D99 (trace ids on the save path; the audit becomes a scheduled job), D101 (a forensic privileged-roster log written on restore).*

### D62 — Analytics: one shared Mixpanel project across Book and Ghost; email identity, Constitution ID + role as user properties
Book and Ghost report to **one Mixpanel project**. Both apps `identify()` on the **email** join key with **Identity Merge** enabled; Book additionally sets **Constitution ID, role, and name as user properties**; every event carries an **`app` discriminator** (`book`/`news`). Taxonomy: SPA page-views on route change plus feature events (search, filter [which field], sort, column-lens, star, "Starred only", "Include deceased", save, photo change, verify, export, import, sync, backup/restore); properties carry context but no PII. `ignore_dnt: true`. A follow-on (in `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS.md`): confirm the live theme's member-identity handling so both halves resolve the same identity.
**Why:** Book and Ghost are one composite system (D54) with real cross-app movement (Linter byline deep-links; a future article-mention list), so one project mirrors that and enables cross-app funnels. The Constitution ID — Book's true person key — cannot be cleanly carried into Ghost's client-readable member object (the pushed name omits `(#5247)` per D55; `note`/`label` are admin-side and not exposed to theme JS), so **email** (already the auth join key) is the distinct_id, with Identity Merge absorbing the rare, propagated email change; setting the Constitution ID and role as **user properties** recovers per-person segmentation without making the ID the key. The `app` property keeps a shared project legible. The Ghost member `uuid` remains an available change-immune key upgrade (D70) if email churn ever matters.

*Later updated by: P6 (composite §12 — event properties carry no PII: no search term, no viewed/starred IDs, no record values), D88 (drops `name`; keeps email/Constitution-ID/role/`ignore_dnt`).*

### D63 — Backups: two-tier; restore is a faithful replacement
**Automated:** a scheduled (daily) timestamped **JSON export of all three collections** to a backup bucket, plus an **image-version manifest**; image objects are protected by GCS object versioning (D8), aged by lifecycle rules — not re-copied each cycle. **Manual:** the Admin "Download backup" (D52) produces an archive of the JSON **plus a zip of current image objects**. **Restore** (typed-ack, D52) is a **faithful whole-database replacement** — it restores system/internal and verification fields verbatim and is **not** subject to the import rules (D68), since its semantics are "be exactly this snapshot," not "merge corrections."
**Why:** The two stores need different handling; versioning already protects images, so re-zipping them nightly is wasteful while a manifest pins the right versions for restore. The manual archive gives an off-platform copy. Restore is the most destructive operation in Book (it replaces everything), which is why D52 gates it behind the strongest confirmation.

*Later updated by: D68 (restore stays exempt from the import rules), D101 (offline restore + structural validation + forensic roster log), D102 (continuous backup-integrity verification).*

### D64 — TLS is Google-managed; no manually-provisioned certificates
On Firebase Hosting + Cloud Run + Cloud CDN, certificates are **Google-managed and auto-renewing** (no certbot, no renewal cron). Book adds forced HTTP→HTTPS redirection, an **HSTS** header, and the already-`Secure`/`HttpOnly`/`SameSite=Strict` session cookie (§2.3) and CDN signed cookie (§2.5).
**Why:** The chosen serverless stack (D10) provides managed TLS, superseding any self-hosted-server certificate model; certificate provisioning follows automatically once the custom domain verifies.

*Later updated by: D107 (the header set grows from HSTS-only to a strict CSP + `nosniff` + framing + `Referrer-Policy`), D126 (managed TLS is on Firebase Hosting + Cloud Run only — there is no Cloud CDN or load-balancer certificate).*

### D65 — Testing: Vitest + Firestore emulator; "test non-trivial logic"; Playwright E2E; a committed deterministic fake-data generator
Unit/integration tests use **Vitest** (one tool across the shared TS), with store-touching code run against the **Firestore emulator**. Governing principle: **test anything with non-trivial logic** (branching, transformation, parsing, derivation, state machines) — broader than high-risk-only, narrower than coverage-for-its-own-sake; the priority list (DATABASE-SCHEMA §8 validation, the per-role projection, auth/JWKS, the `IdentityProvider` seam, CDN-cookie, big-brother cycle check, Canonical Name, the query engine, the verification/import coupling, OCC, sync push, CSV export/import, the image pipeline, address/class-year/majors logic, stars, the reconciliation audit, backup/restore, the roster endpoint, URL/state) is tiered by risk. **E2E uses Playwright** (the committed suite; the browser MCPs are for interactive verification). A **deterministic, seeded fake-data generator** (committed generator + small fixtures, **not** a generated blob) produces ~600–2,000 obviously-fake profiles spanning every feature; it is an **early build deliverable** and never touches production. Methodology lives in `ENGINEERING-DESIGN` §6.6; sequencing and CI gating live in `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN.md`.
**Why:** Vitest fits the Vite/TS stack with minimal dependencies; the emulator gives faithful query/concurrency behavior for free. The "non-trivial logic" rule answers the coverage question honestly — test where a bug is plausible and the type system won't catch it. A committed deterministic generator is reproducible and reviewable without diff noise, and building it first unblocks testing everything else.

### D66 — Phonetic-matching A/B is a build-time experiment with a recall-favoring criterion
The Double Metaphone vs. Beider-Morse choice (D35) is decided by an **offline evaluation harness** (both from the talisman library) over a curated set of (query → expected-match) pairs built from the expected error classes (transliteration variants, sound-alikes, hyphenated/middle-name cases), seeded from the fake dataset plus hand-picked international names. The decision **favors recall** provided precision stays tolerable; index size and latency are tie-breakers only. The chosen algorithm is recorded when search is built.
**Why:** The choice depends on measured behavior over realistic name-confusion cases, not a-priori reasoning; for a small directory, missing a brother is worse than a couple of extra near-matches. Fixing the method and criterion now lets the experiment run cleanly at implementation time.

### D67 — Accessibility is verified in three layers
WCAG 2.1 AA (D32) is **verified, not asserted**: automated **`@axe-core/playwright`** failing CI on violations; CI **contrast-gating** of the AA palette; and a **manual per-page checklist** (keyboard order = reading order, visible focus, modal focus trap, screen-reader smoke test, `prefers-reduced-motion`, 200%/largest-font usability). Because Radix supplies a11y by construction (D29), most effort guards the custom surfaces (chips, grid, virtualized list).
**Why:** Automated tools catch only part of the AA surface; the rest needs human checks. Verification keeps the AA *policy* honest rather than aspirational.

*Later updated by: D79 (target raised to WCAG 2.2 AA; new success criteria folded into the checklist), U6 (composite §12 — virtualized-grid ARIA checks added), D111 (help wiring split across phases along the WCAG line).*

### D68 — Bulk-CSV import is a per-row non-owner edit; verification fields are import-ignored (resolves the D28 open question)
The steady-state admin CSV import is treated as a **per-row non-owner edit**: a row whose content actually changes **unverifies** that profile (clearing `lastVerifiedDate`/`verifiedBy`); unchanged rows and deceased profiles keep their status. The verification fields are **system-managed and ignored on import** (they may still be *exported* as read-only reporting columns). The **dry-run preview** reports "update X / add Y / **unverify Z**." The one-time pre-launch bulk-loader (D57) is exempt — it is the genesis load.
**Why:** Realistic imports are externally-vetted MITAA-sourced corrections — non-owner changes by definition — so unverifying changed rows keeps the "verified" badge honest (the D28 invariant), while ignoring the verification columns forecloses asserting verification over content no owner confirmed. The dry-run surfaces the verification blast radius before Apply.

*Later updated by: D100 (the steady-state MITAA bulk-CSV import deferred to the post-MVP backlog).*

### D69 — Majors vocabulary maintained by a checked-in source file + seed/reconcile script; runtime UI deferred (resolves the carried Session-2 item)
The `majors` controlled vocabulary (D14) is a **version-controlled source file** applied by a small **idempotent seed/reconcile script** that doubles as the initial loader and the steady-state maintenance path. A runtime admin curation UI is deferred to post-MVP.
**Why:** The MIT course list changes rarely, so a CRUD UI is poor MVP value; a checked-in file keeps the vocabulary reviewable in git, and the script removes the footgun of hand-editing production documents (a mistyped code silently breaks every profile that references it).

### D70 — `ghostMemberId` is the Ghost Admin-API member id; `ghostMemberUuid` is captured alongside (corrects D56)
Book stores **two** system/internal Ghost identifiers: **`ghostMemberId`** = the Ghost **Admin-API member id**, the handle used to address Book → Ghost member `PUT`/`DELETE` updates (D55); and **`ghostMemberUuid`** = the member **UUID**, captured alongside it and reserved for future use (e.g. a cross-app analytics identity, D62). Both are excluded from every client projection and from the CSV. D56 described the stored field as the "UUID," but Ghost's Admin API addresses members by **id**, not uuid.
**Why:** D55's update addressing requires the **id**, while a future client-readable join (such as uuid-keyed Mixpanel identity) would need the **uuid**; both are returned together on member create, so capturing both now is free and keeps that option open without re-touching Ghost. This corrects a latent inconsistency between D55 (addresses by the stored id) and D56 (called it the uuid).

*Later updated by: D81 (capture of `ghostMemberUuid` deferred and removed from MVP until a concrete consumer exists; supersedes the "capture both now" rationale).*

### D71 — Firestore schema migration: code-defaulting for additive changes, versioned idempotent scripts for breaking ones
Schema evolution is **tiered**. **Additive / optional changes need no migration** — the read path and the shared validation module (D50) supply defaults for fields absent on older documents. **Breaking / structural changes** get **numbered, idempotent migration scripts**: checked into the repo, dry-run-able, emulator-tested with the fake data, run against production **only after a backup** (D63), logged to the audit stream (D61), and ordered by a global `_meta` **schema-version marker**. **Eager batch migration** suits Book's ~2,000-document scale (seconds to run; the snapshot listener, D26, propagates it); lazy per-document on-read migration is the escape hatch. These **recurring** scripts live in the repo, distinct from the one-time pre-launch loaders (D57).
**Why:** Firestore is schemaless but Book's shared `Profile` type (D3) is strict, so stored data drifts as the type evolves during debugging and future features. Most changes are additive and free; the rare breaking change needs a safe, repeatable, restore-reversible procedure rather than ad-hoc console edits.

*Later updated by: D100 (R15 execution-hardening folded in — run in-network with cursor-resume; drain the old revision before migrating).*

### D72 — Pre-launch system/UAT testing on a staging Book via a `DevIdentityProvider`; the real Ghost bridge tested free, the write path isolated
Pre-launch **system/acceptance testing** runs on a **separate staging Book** (its own project / Firestore / bucket) loaded with **fake data only**. UAT uses a **`DevIdentityProvider`** — the test implementation of the `IdentityProvider` seam (D21) — for **Ghost-free, role-switchable login** (also used by local dev and the Playwright suite), **env-gated so it cannot run in production**. The real Ghost **auth/read bridge** is tested at full fidelity by an **additive staging route on the real Ghost Pro** (no second instance, no disruption to the live newsletter); the verification logic is also unit-tested with controlled test keys. The Book → Ghost **write path** must **never** point at production Ghost from staging — it is stubbed/logged for UAT, with a short-lived self-hosted/trial Ghost reserved for a faithful write run. Staging bring-up and the UAT phase are sequenced in `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN.md`.
**Why:** Separating "test Book's features" from "test the Ghost handshake" dissolves the apparent need for a paid second Ghost Pro: the D21 seam makes UAT Ghost-free and safe (fake data, no real members at risk), the read-path bridge is additive and free on the live Pro, and only the member-write path needs a non-production Ghost — which must be isolated from production to avoid mutating live members. The dev provider also speeds local development and E2E, so it pays for itself beyond staging.

*Later updated by: D102 (staging becomes on-demand/ephemeral, script-provisioned), D108 (`DevIdentityProvider` compiled out of the prod bundle + CI assertion + startup alert, beyond the single env gate).*

## Interstitial Session — Performance & Delivery Optimizations (2026-06-07)

A short discussion-only session, between Sessions 6b and 6c, on three wire-efficiency levers: browser caching of the code, over-the-wire compression of the dataset, and shrinking the JS bundle. The organizing constraint, recorded once here and referenced by the decisions below: **a meaningful fraction of the brotherhood are older members (many 70+) who are technically capable but on slow connections** — an address list heavy with `@aol.com`-era domains, and (from the editor's prior experience with a similar cohort) some still on DSL who complain, loudly, about slow page loads. These optimizations are therefore worth doing in MVP rather than banked as deferred, and they pair with the existing accessibility-as-policy posture (D32) as deliberate accommodations of that audience. All four are decided as **MVP**.

### D73 — Browser caching of the SPA bundle: content-hashed immutable assets, no-cache HTML
Vite emits **content-hashed asset filenames**, so each asset URL is immutable by construction. Serve all hashed JS/CSS/asset files with `Cache-Control: public, max-age=31536000, immutable` and the single unhashed `index.html` entry point with `no-cache` / `must-revalidate`, configured in the `firebase.json` headers block. A returning visitor re-downloads only the chunks whose hash actually changed; the always-revalidated HTML — which references the current hashes — guarantees no stale-bundle/fresh-API skew across deploys. **No service worker / offline-PWA caching** (considered and declined indefinitely): persisting member contact data on-device carries privacy and staleness costs unjustified for an online-only directory.
**Why:** The code changes seldom, so caching it in the browser spares repeat downloads on every visit to `book.pbe400.org` — meaningful for the slow-connection cohort above — while content-hash immutability plus no-cache HTML makes that caching both automatic (a Vite default plus a few lines of Hosting config) and safe.

*Later updated by: D112 (a server-advertised client version prompts a stale already-open tab to refresh across a deploy).*

### D74 — Keep the JS bundle small: minification, tree-shaking, and route/feature code-splitting
Rely on Vite's production **minification** (esbuild) and Rollup **tree-shaking** (both on by default); import narrowly (e.g. per-icon `lucide-react` imports, not barrel imports) so unused code is dropped; and **code-split the initial load** so the **Directory** — the page every user lands on — ships lean, lazy-loading the heavier, rarely-first-touched surfaces: the **Admin page**, the **react-easy-crop headshot modal** (D47), and the **talisman phonetic-matching libraries** (D35). A **CI bundle-size budget** (e.g. `rollup-plugin-visualizer` plus a size check) guards against a heavyweight dependency creeping in, consistent with the project's no-unnecessary-dependencies value.
**Why:** First paint on a slow link is dominated by bytes shipped. Minification and tree-shaking are free toolchain defaults (the only failure mode is shipping a dev build); the substantive choice is splitting the cold-path code off the Directory bundle, which directly shrinks the first load the slow-connection cohort waits on. Compounds with D73 (Firebase Hosting also compresses static assets over the wire, so the bundle ships minified *and* brotli'd).

### D75 — Over-the-wire compression of the bulk dataset: brotli primary, gzip fallback, memoized and invalidated on write
`GET /api/profiles` is compressed **at the application** — Cloud Run does not transparently compress responses, unlike Firebase Hosting on the static side. The backend serves **brotli at maximum quality (level 11)** to clients advertising `Accept-Encoding: br` and **gzip** to those that don't, via the standard `Content-Encoding` / `Vary: Accept-Encoding` negotiation; the **browser decompresses transparently**, so the frontend `fetch()` only ever sees JSON. Compression is **lazy and memoized**: a write — local, or arriving via the snapshot listener (D26) — **invalidates** the cached compressed buffer rather than rebuilding it, and the next request (re)compresses and caches. The **brotli** buffer is built on first demand; the **gzip** buffer is built only if a non-brotli client actually appears (≈10 ms, once, then reused). This layers above the always-hot in-memory dataset cache (D7) — only the *compressed projected payload* is lazily derived from it. The **Firestore↔backend hop is not compressed**: it is rare (cold start only), intra-datacenter, and billed per-operation not per-byte, so there is nothing to gain.
**Why:** The profiles payload — ~2,000 records of repeated JSON keys — compresses ~80–90% under gzip and somewhat better under brotli (roughly 15–25% smaller again), and it gates app startup, making it the single highest-leverage byte reduction available for the slow-connection cohort. The lazy-memoize design avoids wasted recompression both during an admin's edit burst (writes only invalidate; nobody pays until a download is actually requested) and in the common single-user-already-loaded case (the user holds the data in memory and never re-requests it) — and, because the one compression is amortized over every subsequent download, it specifically unlocks the slowest/smallest brotli-11 setting a per-request server could never afford. Compression also forecloses exotic payload schemes (short/columnar keys, a binary format): the repeated field names that look wasteful are exactly what the codec eliminates, so readable JSON stays essentially as small without the code complexity.

*Later updated by: D84 (compression moved off the event loop; brotli-11 precomputed on write into the snapshot).*

### D76 — Conditional GET (ETag) on the bulk dataset so unchanged data isn't re-sent
`GET /api/profiles` carries an **`ETag`** derived from the dataset's latest Firestore `updateTime` (already tracked for optimistic concurrency, D25), with `Cache-Control: private, no-cache` so the browser caches the body but always revalidates, and shared/edge caches never store the per-role payload. A returning or reloading browser then automatically attaches `If-None-Match`; if the dataset is unchanged the backend replies **`304 Not Modified` with an empty body** and the browser serves its cached copy. This is **browser-managed HTTP caching** — the SPA code does nothing; it simply receives JSON. The scope is honestly bounded: it helps a *repeat or reload* load on unchanged data (common for a frequent visitor, since the data changes seldom), **not** the first load of a fresh session, and not within a session (where the dataset is already held in memory and never re-requested).
**Why:** It is nearly free — the `updateTime` token already exists and the browser does the rest — and it spares the repeat-visit-on-slow-link user even the compressed payload, the same don't-re-download-what-hasn't-changed instinct as the bundle caching (D73), applied to the data rather than the code. The browser's HTTP cache survives tab close and restart (unlike Book's in-memory copy), which is what makes a later revisit benefit.

*Later updated by: D82 (the ETag keyed by role/projection identity — composite S6), D85 (the snapshot stays backend-internal, served only via `/api/profiles`), D95 (read-side conditional-GET caching superseded by `no-store` to keep no PII on disk).*

---

## Triage (post-design-review) — Session 0: Gating Decisions (2026-06-08)

The design suite was reviewed by two clean-context LLM reviewers (`history/DESIGN-REVIEW-COMPOSITE.md`, 81 findings) and the findings are being triaged in sessions per `history/TRIAGE-PLAN.md`. Session 0 settled the scoping forks that govern everything downstream; its brief is `history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-0.md`. These are the first decisions numbered above D77.

### D77 — PBE is not a CCPA "business"; Book builds no privacy-compliance machinery and has no record-deletion / "tombstone" requirement
The editor determined that PBE — a nonprofit fraternal/alumni group — meets **none** of CCPA's "business" thresholds (≈$25M revenue; buy/sell/share the PI of 100,000+ consumers; or ≥50% of revenue from selling/sharing PI), and CCPA regulates only for-profit "businesses." CCPA therefore does **not** bind PBE, and no further legal review is planned (the thresholds are unambiguous). Book accordingly builds **no** CCPA-compliance machinery: no DSAR/access workflow, no deletion-request workflow, no purge-from-backups/versions/Mixpanel lifecycle, no retention schedule, no first-login interstitial. **No "tombstone" / deleted-ID deny-list is built either.** Reasoning: Book's only *required* data is the immutable Constitution signature-page fact (full name, class year, Constitution ID — the signing number); every other field is brother-editable. A brother can never have his *record* deleted on request (membership is a historical signed fact, not erasable), and whole-record deletion is an **admin error-correction action only** (duplicates, records created in error) — so there is no "deleted person" for a restore to resurrect. A brother emptying a field (e.g., removing an address) is a value change, not a deletion; preserving such post-snapshot changes across a restore would require full point-in-time versioning of the database, which restore's "be exactly this snapshot" semantics (D63) deliberately are **not**. **Retained on independent merit (not as compliance):** the existing PBE privacy notice — which already names Mixpanel, MITAA, and Ghost — is made **publicly reachable without login** (**done 2026-06-08** — now public at `https://pbe400.org/privacy/`) and **linked from Book**, including the login/landing surface (a build-time item, pending Book), so notice precedes the first-sign-in auto-provisioning. Mixpanel payload minimization and the `ignore_dnt: true` setting (P1) remain a **values** decision for the privacy-cluster triage (Session 3), not a legal one.
**Why:** Settling applicability up front scopes the legal force of the whole privacy-blocker cluster (P1/P2/P3/P5/P8) before those findings are triaged. Separating "legal mandate" from "worth doing anyway" keeps the design honest: the heavyweight compliance apparatus earns nothing for a non-business at PBE's scale, while the one genuinely worthwhile item — a discoverable notice that discloses third-party egress — is cheap and correct regardless of law. Dropping the tombstone also **removes a cross-session dependency** the triage plan had assumed (TRIAGE-PLAN §4: "P3 defines the tombstone, which R5/R1 consume"): R1's create path is made safe by idempotent create (address by `ghostMemberId`, treat "already exists" as success), and R5's restore→Ghost divergence is caught by the existing read-only reconciliation audit (D55) — neither needs a deny-list. Consequently P3 and P8 are pre-framed toward **(b) as-designed** when Session 3 disposes them.

*Later updated by: D116 (a persistent footer link to the same privacy notice inside the authenticated app, pinning the position D77 left to the login/landing surface).*

### D78 — Linter roster auth: in-code Google-JWKS verification with issuer + audience + subject pinned; the Cloud Run IAM "front door" is dropped
D58 offered two co-equal authentications for `GET /api/roster`: verify the Google service-account OIDC token **(A)** at the Cloud Run "front door" (require IAM auth + grant the `linter` SA `roles/run.invoker`) or **(B)** in-code against Google's JWKS. Option A is **infeasible as architected** and is removed: the public sign-in endpoint `/api/auth/session` must be invocable **unauthenticated** (it is how a signed-out brother establishes a session) and sits on the **same Cloud Run service** as `/api/roster`; Cloud Run's `run.invoker` IAM is enforced **per service**, with no per-path option, so requiring IAM on the roster would also lock out sign-in (and `/api/profiles`). Book therefore mandates the **in-code** check: verify the token against Google's JWKS requiring **issuer = Google**, **audience = Book**, **and subject = the exact `linter` service account** — the subject pin (absent from the original wording) is essential, since issuer+audience alone would accept *any* Google-issued token for that audience. The service stays publicly invocable (it must, for auth), so the in-code subject check — not `run.invoker` — is the control; the `linter` SA stays least-privileged. Tests assert wrong-issuer / wrong-audience / wrong-subject are all rejected. Amends D58; updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.2` and `API-SPEC §8` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** This resolves the review's one outright factual disagreement (composite S17): Reviewer B's per-service-IAM claim is correct and load-bearing, while Reviewer A's subject-pin is a real hardening gap on whichever option survives. The in-code path was already the runtime-agnostic co-equal option and mirrors the Ghost-JWKS verification the app already performs, and it preserves the credited keyless service-account strength. The only alternative that could keep an IAM front door — splitting the roster onto a separate Cloud Run service with its own IAM — adds a second service, deploy, and cache for no benefit over the in-code check, and is rejected.

### D79 — Accessibility target raised from WCAG 2.1 AA to 2.2 AA
Book's accessibility policy (D32) and its three-layer verification (D67) now target **WCAG 2.2 AA** (published Oct 2023, a strict superset of 2.1) rather than 2.1 AA. The five new A/AA success criteria are folded into the D67 manual checklist: **2.4.11 Focus Not Obscured (Minimum, AA)** and **3.3.7 Redundant Entry (A)** add genuine new checks (relevant to the sticky-header virtualized grid and the multi-step edit/import flows); **2.5.8 Target Size (Minimum, AA)** is **already met** (D32 commits ≥44×44px, far above the 24×24 minimum); **3.3.8 Accessible Authentication (Minimum, AA)** is **already satisfied** by the Ghost magic-link bridge (no cognitive-function test); **3.2.6 Consistent Help (A)** dovetails with the in-context help work (D53). The obsolete **4.1.1 Parsing** (removed in 2.2) is dropped from the checklist. Updates `PRD §5.5` and `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.6` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** Targeting the superseded standard for a 2026 accessibility-first launch was simply an un-revisited default; 2.2's additions are squarely relevant to the older-alum audience, and the cost is low because the verification machinery (D67) already exists and Book already meets two of the five new criteria. Consistent with the editor's accessibility-as-policy posture.

---

## Triage — Session 1: Fast-track ratification (2026-06-08)

The seventeen fast-track findings (C1, C2, C3, C6, C8, C10, C11, C12, C13, C14, P6, P15, R16, R17, R19, R20, U6) were all disposed **(a) fix**; brief: `history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-1.md`. Most are stale-reference, traceability, or implementation-pin corrections recorded only in the composite §12 worksheet and applied to the delivered docs in the resolution pass. Two carried foundational decisions, numbered here:

### D80 — Un-marking deceased restores the consent/verification state captured at mark-time
Marking a brother deceased forces both email-consent flags (`allowNewsletterEmail`, `allowCommentReplyEmail`) off and freezes verification (D28/D48). Because the deceased flag is **reversible for admin error-correction** (D49), the system now **snapshots** the prior consent flags and verification state (`lastVerifiedDate`/`verifiedBy`) at the moment of marking, and **restores that snapshot** on un-marking, rather than leaving consent silently off and verification frozen. The snapshot is cleared once restored. Records composite **C14** (a); updates `DATABASE-SCHEMA §8` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** The email-consent flag is not a passive preference — it is pushed to Ghost's mailing-list subscription state and directly governs whether Ghost emails the brother newsletters, so a mistaken mark-deceased silently **unsubscribes a living brother from real mail**. Only restoring the prior values fully reverses that real-world effect. The privacy concern that would otherwise argue against auto-restoring consent (re-enabling email a brother never consented to) is moot in the error case: the reversal restores the brother's own prior choice, not an invented one. Without the snapshot, un-marking is a silent, unrecoverable consent-data loss on what is explicitly meant to be a reversible correction.

*Later updated by: D115 (the mark-time snapshot/restore pattern reused to make de-brothering reversible); N68 (2026-07-08 — `allowCommentReplyEmail` removed, so the force-off and the snapshot now cover only `allowNewsletterEmail`).*

### D81 — `ghostMemberUuid` capture is deferred until a concrete consumer exists (supersedes D70) *(Linear: OFC-174)*
D70 chose to capture and store `ghostMemberUuid` now ("free to grab," reserved for a possible future cross-app analytics identity), even though no MVP feature reads it — only `ghostMemberId` is actually used (for Ghost-update addressing, D55). `ghostMemberUuid` is **removed from the MVP schema, projection, and backup**, and its capture is **deferred** until a concrete need (e.g. the Mixpanel-uuid-identity idea, D62/D70) is actually decided. Re-capture later is trivial — it is a single field, re-derivable from Ghost. Supersedes D70; records composite **P15** (a); updates `DATABASE-SCHEMA §3.1` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** D77 makes data minimization a **policy** posture (build to the stricter standard even though CCPA does not bind PBE); an identifier collected with no current consumer is precisely what minimization says to drop, and removing it shrinks the projection, backup, and migration surface for no functional loss.

---

## Triage — Session 2: Read / cache / compute (2026-06-09)

The seven read-path findings (S1, S6, R2, R3, R6, R8, S10) — the suite's hardest interdependency cluster, anchored on the in-memory cache + brotli memoization (D26/D75/D76) and Cloud Run's scale-to-zero behavior — were all disposed **(a) fix**; brief: `history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-2.md`. The cluster turned on one architectural choice — capping Cloud Run at a single instance (**D83**) — that dissolved the hardest finding (R2) at its root and reshaped the rest. **S6** is recorded as an amendment to D76 (no new number); the other six carry foundational decisions, numbered here.

### D82 — The bulk read is split: a per-role projection of all records (only the brother buffer cached) plus a separate self-record fetch
The single memoized `/api/profiles` compressed buffer keyed only by encoding (D75) served the first post-write caller's **owner-aware** projection to every subsequent caller — so if an admin or manager was the first GET after any write, brothers downloading next received admin/manager-only fields and `adminNote` (composite **S1**, the review's most serious security finding). The read is split: `GET /api/profiles` returns a **uniform per-role projection of all records** (every record at the caller's role visibility, including a plain role-projection of the caller themselves), and the caller's own **full** record (own off-toggle values; never their own `adminNote`) is delivered by a separate self-record fetch (`GET /api/me`) that the SPA overlays. Because the per-*user*-ness of the old payload came entirely from "your own record in full," factoring it out makes the bulk payload **identical for all callers of the same role** — correct and cacheable. Only the **brother-role** buffer is precomputed and cached (the ≈700-person audience, where amortization pays); **manager and admin projections are computed fresh per request** (few callers, poor amortization, and the conditional-GET/304 path already covers their reloads), compressed off the event loop at a moderate level. A test asserts no two callers can receive each other's projection — the cross-caller isolation case absent from the §6.6 plan. Records composite **S1** (a); updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.4`/`§1.6`/`§2.4`, `API-SPEC §1`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §9`, and the `§6.6` test plan in the resolution pass.
**Why:** This preserves and *sharpens* the credited strength of server-side projection as the single enforcement point (composite §9) rather than dismantling it, and it restores D75's amortization economics — a handful of role buffers (in practice one hot brother buffer) instead of ~700 per-caller variants, which would have negated the memoization rationale and worsened R3. It also yields a minimization bonus under D77: the bulk payload no longer carries anyone's own-private values at all. Keying the ETag by the same projection identity resolves S6 in the same stroke.

### D83 — Cloud Run is capped at one instance (`max-instances = 1`, scale-to-zero): the single instance's cache is authoritative, retiring the multi-instance coherence model
D26 claimed the in-memory cache was "self-healing and correct at any instance count" via a background Firestore snapshot listener — but on scale-to-zero Cloud Run, idle instances are CPU-throttled, so a background listener can stall or drop without redelivery; and because `GET /api/profiles` is served only from cache and its ETag derives from that cache, a stalled listener would serve stale data **and 304-confirm it** (composite **R2**, the suite's most serious un-addressed operational assumption). Book resolves this by **capping `max-instances = 1`** while keeping **scale-to-zero** (`min-instances = 0`): with at most one instance there is no second writer, so the single instance — which already updates its own cache synchronously on every write (§1.5) and is the only path through which writes flow — is **authoritative by construction**. The snapshot listener is **retained only as a convergence safety net** for the brief two-instance window a deploy creates (old + new revision overlap) and for out-of-band writers (restore/migration, handled in Session 4); it is no longer the correctness mechanism, and the idle-CPU concern is moot because in steady state nothing else changes Firestore. Deploys are configured to **cut traffic over to the new revision immediately** (not gradually) so writes stop hitting the draining instance, and a **listener-health watchdog** (resume-token / last-event age) plus a **cache-age / listener-disconnect alert** remain as cheap insurance (feeds R10). Supersedes D26's "correct at any instance count" framing; updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.5` and `§6.1` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** This dissolves R2's failure mode at its root (no multiplicity → no divergence) for **$0**, instead of buying an always-warm instance (`min-instances ≥ 1` + CPU-always-allocated), which the editor priced at ≈**$180/year** — a substantial sum for a volunteer-run nonprofit, and unjustified given the scale: at ≈700 living brothers with bursty bi-annual traffic, one instance at default concurrency is far more capacity than will ever be needed. The conscious tradeoff is explicit — **Book uses Cloud Run for its scale-to-zero cost floor (vs. an always-on VM), not for autoscaling** — and the single-instance ceiling sacrifices horizontal scale-out headroom, accepted as a deliberate, trivially reversible config choice. Write-through Firestore (D7) means the deploy/crash windows lose no data; the cold starts scale-to-zero retains are made cheap by D85 (one-read hydration) and D84 (precomputed buffer), and raise the importance of D87 (JWKS persistence).

### D84 — `/api/profiles` compression moves off the request path: brotli-11 precomputed on write into the snapshot, with debounced and explicit batch regeneration (amends D75)
D75 ran a full-dataset **brotli level 11** compression synchronously on the single-threaded Node event loop on the first request after any write or cold start — a latency spike for the unlucky caller and, under repeated edits, a low-privilege CPU DoS that blocks all requests (composite **R3**). Compression now runs **off the event loop**: on write, the instance recomputes the brother-role projection and brotli-11-compresses it on the libuv threadpool (async `zlib.brotliCompress`), swapping the buffer in atomically and writing it into the GCS snapshot (D85); a cold-starting instance therefore **loads** a ready-to-serve buffer rather than computing one. Because compression now happens only on the rare write path, **brotli-11 is retained** (maximum ratio for the slow-connection cohort, D73–D76) with no cold-start or request-path penalty. Rebuilds are coalesced two ways: a time-based **debounce** for organic write bursts, and an explicit **"regenerate once after the batch"** hook that known-batch operations (bulk-CSV apply, bulk delete) invoke after all rows are written — so a 500-row import triggers one recompression, not 500 (coordinated with the Session-4 bulk-ops design). Manager/admin projections, computed fresh per request (D82), are compressed async at a **moderate level (~brotli-5)**, with gzip as the fallback for clients not advertising `br`. Compression time at ~2,000 records is bounded and measured as a Phase-7 performance gate. Amends D75; updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.6` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** Moving compression to the threadpool removes brotli-11's only real cost (event-loop blocking / cold-start latency) while keeping its byte savings — the best outcome for the audience the product is built around. Precomputing into the snapshot means the now-common cold start (a consequence of the scale-to-zero choice, D83) pays nothing for compression. The explicit batch hook prevents the bulk-operation amplification a pure time-debounce could still let through.

### D85 — The denormalized all-profiles snapshot is pulled into MVP as a backend-internal GCS object read on cold start (promotes the PRD §3.2 deferral)
With scale-to-zero retained (D83), cold starts are common, and each previously re-read all ~2,000 profile documents from Firestore and recompressed on a cold instance — adding seconds precisely for the slow-connection cohort, especially during the post-newsletter traffic burst (composite **R8**). The deferred "single denormalized all-profiles snapshot" (PRD §3.2) is **pulled into MVP**: a **GCS object** (not a Firestore document — the raw dataset can exceed the 1 MiB doc limit) holding the **raw dataset, the precomputed brother-role brotli buffer (D84), and the dataset version/ETag**, **regenerated by the Cloud Run backend on every write**. A cold-starting instance hydrates from this **one GCS read** instead of ~2,000 Firestore reads and can serve the brother buffer immediately. The snapshot is **backend-internal**: clients never read it directly — profile data is always served through `GET /api/profiles`, which enforces the per-role projection and session auth and runs the ETag/304 logic, and the bulk payload stays `private, no-cache` (D76). (Only headshots are served direct from GCS/CDN to the client, via the signed cookie of D23/§2.5.) The stale-snapshot risk — a write that commits to Firestore but fails to refresh the snapshot — is handled by reconciling the snapshot's version against Firestore's latest on cold start and falling back to a full read on divergence (a robustness detail for the resolution pass). Records composite **R8** (a), promoting the PRD §3.2 deferral into MVP; updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.5` and removes the item from `PRD §3.2` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** The optimization is cheap, already designed, and aimed squarely at the cohort the product bends over backwards for, at the exact moment latency matters most — a cold instance serving the first click-through after a newsletter send. Keeping it backend-internal preserves server-side projection as the single enforcement point and D76's private/no-cache posture: the data's access control is never delegated to GCS object ACLs.

*Later updated by: D126 (headshots are served by the app from the private bucket, not direct from GCS/CDN to the client — the backend-internal dataset snapshot of this decision is unaffected).*

### D86 — Rate limits and abuse controls on auth, writes, and the bulk read (new policy)
No throttling was specified anywhere in the suite (composite **S10**). Book adds **rate limits / concurrency caps** on the authentication endpoint, on mutating endpoints, and on the bulk read; **single-flights and caps** unknown-`kid` JWKS refetches (shared with D87); and **debounces** compression rebuilds (shared with D84). Two of S10's three named amplifiers are already neutralized by other Session-2 decisions — the brotli-recompression CPU DoS by D84 (compression off the event loop, debounced) and the unknown-`kid` JWKS-refetch lever by D87 (single-flight) — and the single-instance cap (D83) further contains a CPU-exhaustion attempt to one instance; S10 adds the remaining standard throttling, including a cap on the unauthenticated crypto work in `POST /api/auth/session`. Records composite **S10** (a); updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.7` and `§6.1` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** Detection (the §6.1 sign-in-denial alert) is not prevention; explicit limits bound both accidental and malicious load, and matter more now that a single instance (D83) serves everyone.

*Implemented (2026-07-02, Phase-1 retro review OFC-73). The policy was asserted here but not yet built; this closes the gap. Concretely: `@fastify/rate-limit` is registered opt-in (`global: false`) and applied per route (`apps/api/src/security/rate-limit.ts`) — the unauthenticated auth path (`/api/auth/start`, `/api/auth/session`) is IP-keyed (30/min; `trustProxy` gives the real client IP behind Firebase Hosting → Cloud Run, D126), and the bulk read (60/min) and every mutation (edits, stars, exports, "View as"; 120/min) are **session-keyed**, so one caller cannot starve the single instance and members are not lumped into one shared-proxy bucket. The compression-CPU amplifier the finding also named is fixed at the source: the manager/admin projections, previously reprojected and brotli-compressed on **every** `GET /api/profiles`, are now memoized in `ProfileCache` and invalidated on write (the brother payload is already precomputed off the request path, D84). The IP-layer bound is deliberately coarse — a real DoS shield would need the load balancer/Cloud Armor D126 removed to floor cost — so the session-keyed limits are the load-bearing control. Still separate and as previously scoped: the JWKS single-flight (D87) and the debounced off-event-loop compression rebuild (D84, Phase 7).*

### D87 — JWKS is persisted/seeded across cold starts so sign-in survives a Ghost blip (load-bearing under scale-to-zero)
JWT verification needs Ghost's JWKS, cached **in-memory only**, so a freshly cold-started instance cannot verify any login until it fetches JWKS — and if Ghost/JWKS is unreachable at that moment, **all sign-ins fail** (composite **R6**). Book **persists/seeds the JWKS** (or pins keys) so a cold instance can verify against last-known-good keys for a short grace window during a Ghost outage, and **single-flights and caps** unknown-`kid` refetches (the lever also named in S10/D86). This is the **Ghost** members-API JWKS on the sign-in path — distinct from the **Google** JWKS used for the Linter roster (D78) — but the same single-flight discipline applies to both. Records composite **R6** (a); updates `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.7` in the resolution pass.
**Why:** The scale-to-zero choice (D83) makes cold starts common, which **elevates this from belt-and-suspenders to load-bearing**: without persisted keys, every cold start during even a brief Ghost/JWKS hiccup would lock out all sign-ins. Persisting keys decouples sign-in availability from a momentary JWKS reachability failure at the worst possible time.

---

## Triage — Session 3: Privacy / consent / egress (2026-06-09)

The nineteen privacy-and-egress findings (P1–P17 less the already-disposed P6/P15/P18/P19, plus S9, S12, C5, C7) were triaged; brief: `history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-3.md`. **D77 reshaped the session:** because PBE is not a CCPA "business," the reviewers' four "compliance blockers" (P1/P2/P3/P5/P8) lost their legal force and the session became a set of *values, honesty, and hygiene* calls about data egress rather than a compliance build. **P2** (no DSAR machinery; notice) and **P3** (no deletion/tombstone) are closed by D77 as **(b)/(d)**; **P8** (`adminNote` stays staff-internal) and **P17** (coarse photo grant, D23) are **(b) as-designed**; **P16** is **(b)** with concrete audit/backup retention pinned at **3 months** each (folded into `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.1`/`§6.3` in resolution). **P9** (strict `Referrer-Policy`, landing with the S14 security-headers work in Session 5), **P10** (names-not-values discipline extended to all log streams, amending D61), **S9** (CSV formula-injection neutralization, closing the C4 escaping facet), and **C5** (the manager-set-deceased consent/Ghost side-effect named as the deliberate exception in PRD §4; kept manager-allowed since D80 makes it reversible and it is audited) are **(a) fix** without new numbers. The eight foundational decisions are numbered here.

### D88 — Mixpanel identity payload drops `name`; email `distinct_id`, Constitution ID, role, and `ignore_dnt: true` are all retained
D62 sends email (as `distinct_id`), full name, Constitution ID, and role to Mixpanel on `identify()`, with `ignore_dnt: true` (composite **P1**). The disclosure gap is already closed (D77 — the public notice names Mixpanel), leaving only the *values* question D77 reserved for this session. Resolution: **`name` is dropped**; **email stays the `distinct_id`** (it is the auth + Identity-Merge join key Ghost's own Mixpanel integration uses natively — hashing or uuid-keying would have to be applied identically on both halves and depends on reviving the deferred `ghostMemberUuid`, D81); **Constitution ID and role stay as user properties** (role enables the admin-vs-brother usage segmentation the analytics exists for, with no meaningful privacy downside in a members-only internal tool); and **`ignore_dnt: true` is kept**. Amends D62.
**Why:** Beyond it being an internal, members-only, consensual, now-publicly-disclosed tool: a browser DNT/GPC signal is a *global* "in general, don't let websites track me," not a statement about pbe400.org measuring its own members' use of its own tool — a poor proxy for actual intent. The editor's MITAA precedent makes the over-broad-flag failure concrete: ≈⅓ of brothers checked MITAA's "do not email me," but 100% of those asked meant "no MITAA *fundraising* email," not "don't share with PBE" — treating a blanket signal as a specific objection is exactly that misreading. Dropping `name` is pure minimization (D77 policy) at zero analytics cost. Companion to D62/P6 (the event-property rule that closed the who-searched/viewed/starred-whom leak).

### D89 — MITAA sharing defaults to opt-in; the toggle copy is clarified; the identity/public-death always-flow is retained and disclosed via the notice
`allowShareWithMITAA` (D45/D59) defaulted `true` and migration never seeded it (D57), so passive brothers were silently opted into *external* sharing (composite **P4**); separately the always-flow of name/class/public-death *regardless* of the flag, paired with helper copy implying "off shares none of it," was misleading (composite **P5**). The flag now **defaults `false`** — affirmative opt-in for external sharing — **decoupled** from the peer share-toggles, which keep their open defaults. The two-position switch copy is — **on:** "PBE may occasionally share your contact information with the MIT Alumni Association." / **off:** "PBE will not share your contact information with the MIT Alumni Association." The deliberate **always-flow of identity + public In-Memoriam data is retained** (D59 — it is MIT's own data / the join key, and the reciprocal of the import's mortality-catching value); because "contact information" denotes only the email/phone/postal set the toggle governs, the off-copy is accurate, and the always-flow is disclosed in the **public privacy notice + `USER-MANUAL §8`**, not on the switch. Any use-limitation understanding with MITAA stays **informal** (a low-trust, occasional exchange, D59), not a formal data-processor agreement. Amends D45/D59.
**Why:** D45's open-default nudge is ethical only for data staying inside the brotherhood, by its own stated rationale — which does not cover the one flag whose purpose is to send data *out*; opt-in is the honest floor, and no clean Book→MITAA prior-consent record exists to seed from. The copy fix removes the "shares none" falsehood (the P5 complaint) while keeping the always-flow disclosure where fuller text belongs. CCPA's "no force-transfer after opt-out" objection is moot under D77, so the always-flow survives on its merits.

### D90 — MITAA export is a dedicated, consent-aware admin function, separate from the general CSV export
The MITAA file requires a two-tier selection — identity + public-death for **all** brothers, contact details only for those with `allowShareWithMITAA = true`, emergency **never** (D59) — which a single consent-filtered CSV cannot produce (composite **C7**). Rather than the manual reuse-the-general-export path (D59), Book gains a **second, MITAA-specific admin export** that emits this two-tier file directly, alongside the existing general role-projected export (D41). Formula-injection neutralization (composite S9) applies to its output. Automated MITAA exchange stays deferred (D11); this remains a manual admin operation. Amends D59; couples to D89 (which defines the two tiers).
**Why:** When a manual single-CSV path provably cannot meet a consent invariant ("contact details only for consenting brothers"), the robust answer is a small purpose-built export rather than a fragile hand-run merge; it is admin-only and occasional, so the added surface is minimal.

### D91 — The planned log-reader agent runs first-party / local; no audit-log egress to an external LLM *(Linear: OFC-168)*
§6.1 grants a *planned* log-reader agent `roles/logging.viewer` over the audit stream — the membership's who-did-what-to-whom graph (composite **P12**). The agent is constrained to **first-party / on-premise processing on a local model**; no audit content egresses to an external LLM or SaaS. Its purpose is operational triage of the logs — e.g. "do the logs show behaviors that might indicate the system was compromised?" and "do the logs suggest functions worth optimizing?" — well within reach of a local open-weight model (e.g. a Gemma-class model). If a cloud-hosted model is ever wanted, that becomes a **separate, explicitly-disclosed decision** (notice addendum + field minimization + a processor constraint). The build remains deferred; only the constraint is decided now. Amends D61.
**Why:** This is the most sensitive egress in the suite (behavioral, not contact data) and the one *not* named in the public notice; constraining the agent to local processing forecloses the egress entirely rather than disclosing it, which fits the "data stays in the brotherhood" posture and is feasible given the editor's local-model tooling (the same posture D58 assumes for the Linter).

### D92 — Bulk / MITAA export writes an audit entry via a thin notify endpoint; generation stays client-side
Export is client-side by design (D41) and the §6.1 audit list omits it, so the highest-volume egress — a manager/admin downloading the whole visible directory, or an admin exporting consenting brothers' contacts to MITAA — leaves **no security audit trail** (composite **S12**). The client still **generates** the CSV (D41 preserved), but **notifies a thin backend endpoint** that writes one audit entry (actor, scope, row-count, timestamp); **"export" is added to the §6.1 audit event list**. Amends D41/D61.
**Why:** "Who exported the whole membership, and when" is the event most worth a record for a public-repo directory of real PII, and it is the one egress with no trail; a fire-and-forget ping closes it without dismantling D41's client-side generation (a credited simplicity). The only thing given up is that export is no longer purely offline.

### D93 — Third-party-data consent: emergency contacts default off, a spouse/partner share-toggle is added; the brother's own professional fields stay public
Emergency-contact data (`shareEmergency`) defaulted `true`, broadcasting a non-member's name/phone/email to all brothers and into backups (composite **P13**), and `spousePartnerName` — also a third party's data — had no visibility toggle at all (composite **P14**). **`shareEmergency` now defaults `false`** (opt-in). A **new `shareSpousePartner` toggle** is added to the schema and the Profile UI, **defaulting `false`**. `employerName`, `jobTitle`, and `links` stay **public with no toggle** — they are the brother's own data, voluntarily entered, and an optional field left blank shares nothing. Amends D16/D45.
**Why:** D45's open-default nudge is justified by the data subject being a consenting member sharing his own reachability; it does not reach a *non-member* (a spouse, an emergency contact) who never consented — so the default for their data is off, consistent with Book's own treatment of emergency data as third-party (already excluded from MITAA export). The professional fields need no toggle because the brother controls them by choosing whether to populate them. This grows the consent boolean set again (the seven/three count of C2/D45 moves — reconciled in the resolution pass).

### D94 — Superseded headshot versions are purged after a 3-month lifecycle window
With GCS object versioning on (D8), replacing or removing a headshot left prior versions retrievable (composite **P7**; the URL-*enumeration* facet was already closed by R16's opaque tokens). On replace/remove/delete, prior object versions for that brother are **purged after a 3-month lifecycle window** (aligned with the P16 backup-retention figure), and the CDN is confirmed not to serve superseded objects. Amends D8/D17/D23.
**Why:** A brother who removes his photo reasonably expects it gone; a short retention window honors that while keeping a brief recovery margin (object versions double as image backups, D8). Three months keeps image-version retention in step with the database-backup retention.

*Later updated by: D126 (a removed photo is confirmed gone via the GCS object-version purge and the app/browser cache — there is no CDN to "confirm not serving superseded objects").*

### D95 — `/api/profiles` is served `no-store` (no on-disk PII residue); Book gains a sign-out control
D76 served the bulk read `private, no-cache` so the browser persisted the projected dataset across tab-close for a conditional-GET `304` on a repeat visit — but that persisted copy is **real PII** (every brother's name, plus shared emails/phones) left on a shared/public machine's disk (composite **P11**). Because no browser hook reliably purges the HTTP cache on tab-close, `/api/profiles` is instead served **`no-store`**: the dataset is never written to disk, is held only in memory while the tab is open, and is re-fetched on the next fresh load. This **supersedes D76's read-side conditional-GET caching** (the write-side `If-Match`/`updateTime` OCC of D25 is unaffected). Separately, **Book adds a Sign-out control** (mirroring Ghost's avatar-menu link, top-right) that clears the Book session (and may emit `Clear-Site-Data`) — **reversing D24's "no logout endpoint"** and **resolving U4 ahead of Session 6**. Supersedes D76 (read side); amends D24; resolves U4.
**Why:** The cached payload's PII outweighs D76's cross-session `304` benefit, which was always bounded to a *repeat visit on unchanged data* — and for the typical brother visiting a few times a year the data has usually changed in between, so the `304` rarely fired for him anyway; `no-store`'s re-download cost falls mainly on frequent reloaders (admins/managers), who are least likely to be the slow-connection cohort the caching was for. `no-store` is the only reliable, automatic way to guarantee nothing persists after close — superior to a hidden, easily-forgotten sign-out or an inelegant "remember to log out" admonition. The sign-out control is retained as an expected explicit affordance, not the load-bearing privacy mechanism.

---

## Triage — Session 4 (4a — write seam: Ghost sync & email identity) (2026-06-10)

The first half of Session 4 (the "write seam" cluster) was split from the heavier coherence/restore cluster (4b); brief: `history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-4.md`. The first two decisions, from R1 + S2, reshaped the brief substantially: the async transactional outbox the brief (and Session 0) proposed was **reversed to synchronous Ghost pushes**, and the reservation-*collection* the brief proposed for email uniqueness was replaced by an **in-memory index** — both simplifications enabled by the single-instance choice (D83), both taken on the editor's explicit simplicity-over-added-mechanism mandate. `D##` numbers are assigned in finalization order and reconciled in the resolution pass.

### D96 — Book→Ghost member sync is synchronous (reverses the Session-0 async-outbox direction); sign-in resolves by primary email with a prior-email alias on disagreement
Session 0 directionally chose an async durable outbox for the non-atomic Book→Ghost dual-write (composite **R1**). Live triage **reversed that to synchronous, in-line pushes**: on a write, Book commits Firestore and calls the Ghost Admin API before returning. **Create is Ghost-first** — POST to Ghost, capture `ghostMemberId`, then write Firestore — so a failure cannot strand a brother who can never sign in (the rare reverse, Ghost member created then the Firestore write fails, leaves a *cleanable* Ghost orphan — see R7). **Updates** write Firestore then push; **an email change commits in Book only if Ghost accepts it**, so there is no routine divergence window. On a clear Ghost failure the save reports failure, the user retries, Book stays the system of record, and the read-only reconciliation audit (D55) is the straggler backstop. Pushes are **diff-based** — only the pushed fields that actually changed in a given save are sent — so an unrelated edit never re-asserts a consent value; this specifically prevents silently **re-subscribing a brother who used Ghost's own newsletter unsubscribe link** (that link sets his subscription off in Ghost while Book still reads `true`; a whole-set push would clobber his opt-out — a CAN-SPAM-flavored consent violation). A Ghost-side unsubscribe persists until the brother deliberately re-enables it in Book; the stale Book record is flagged by the reconciliation audit, and whether the audit should *actively* write Book to honor a Ghost-side unsubscribe (vs. report it for admin action) is deferred to Session 4b's reconciliation discussion. This stays within D55's read-only-into-Book invariant (Book is never auto-written from Ghost; diff-push simply declines to clobber Ghost with an unchanged value). **Sign-in resolution:** the Ghost member-session JWT was confirmed to carry only the email (the `sub` claim), so no stable non-email identifier is available and D55's match-on-primary-email stands. The email-change lockout is closed by making auth **tolerant** of a transient Book/Ghost disagreement rather than trying to prevent it: sign-in also accepts the **prior** email until the push is confirmed, via a transient backend-only `pendingPriorEmail` on the profile, folded into the in-memory email index (D97) and set only in the uncertain-outcome case (push timeout / lost acknowledgment), cleared once reconciliation confirms Ghost's email. **Bulk operations remain asynchronous jobs** (Session 4b) that pace their own Ghost pushes within rate limits (D86). **R13** (idempotency) reduces under sync to the lost-response edge: on an uncertain outcome, retry idempotently (address by `ghostMemberId`; re-setting a value is a no-op) and/or read the member back before deciding to commit, and a PATCH treats a conflicting `updateTime` that is its own just-applied write as success. Supersedes the Session-0 R1 direction; amends D55 ("real time on save" → synchronous push, create-Ghost-first, email-commit-gated-on-Ghost); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.1`, `PRD §6.1`.
**Why:** A transactional outbox is the right tool for high-volume or must-not-block external propagation; Book is a low-volume, low-frequency directory with a human almost always present, so immediate feedback + human retry + the existing reconciliation audit is right-sized — and an outbox fights the scale-to-zero choice (D83), since nothing drains a queue while the single instance sleeps without bolting on Cloud Tasks, whereas sync has no queue. Synchronous push also surfaces failures loudly at the moment they occur (answering R10's silent-divergence complaint) and eliminates create-orphans by ordering. The residual non-atomicity no dual-write can avoid (short of distributed transactions Ghost doesn't offer) is made harmless by tolerating transient disagreement at the auth boundary instead of trying to eliminate it.

*Later updated by: D103 (the "stale Book record persists until the brother re-enables it in Book" clause superseded by automatic most-recent-change-wins reconciliation of the newsletter flag), D115 (the Ghost-first create/delete and email-identity model reused to revoke and restore a de-brothered member), N65 (2026-07-08 — the **update** path reversed to **Ghost-first-gated**: Ghost must accept the change before Book commits, unifying every Ghost-touching write under one ordering rule; the `pendingPriorEmail` tolerance **dropped** as no longer needed).*

### D97 — Email uniqueness and normalization are enforced in the single instance's in-memory index; no Firestore reservation collection
Email is the authentication join key but had only `id`-uniqueness and format validation (composite **S2**): a collision — a data-entry duplicate or a brother maliciously editing his email to match an admin's — could authenticate him onto a higher-privilege profile, and case/Unicode drift between Ghost and Book could break legitimate logins. The reviewers' fix was a transactional `emails/{email}` reservation **collection** (the standard pattern for a unique non-key field in a *multi-writer* Firestore app). Live triage chose a simpler mechanism enabled by the single-instance choice (D83): uniqueness is enforced by a **synchronous check-and-claim against an in-memory email→profile index** held by the one authoritative instance and **derived from the profiles themselves** (rebuilt on cold-start hydration from the D85 snapshot). Because Node runs JS single-threaded, the check-and-claim cannot interleave and is atomic within the instance, and there is no separate Firestore structure to drift or leave orphaned (the index is a projection of the durable truth, the profiles). Emails are **normalized destructively on save** — lowercase, trim, Unicode-NFC — and only the normalized form is stored; the same normalization is applied to the JWT `sub` at resolution, closing the drift facet. No provider-specific (Gmail dot/plus) normalization. The index spans **primary and `alternateEmail` in one shared namespace** (no address appears twice anywhere in Book). **Resolution fails closed:** if a normalized email ever resolves to more than one profile, deny sign-in rather than guess; with the in-memory claim, post-launch duplicates cannot be created, so fail-closed is the backstop for a duplicate slipped in by the genesis load or a migration (it surfaces as a sign-in denial until an admin de-dups). The brief two-revision deploy overlap D83 permits is not cross-instance-atomic, but the chance of two concurrent same-email writes in that window is negligible and fail-closed covers it; uniqueness enforcement for out-of-band writers (genesis load, bulk import, restore) is a Session-4b item. The same index carries D96's `pendingPriorEmail` alias. Records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.1`/`§5.1`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §8`/`§3.1`, `API-SPEC §2`.
**Why:** D83's single writer makes a separate reservation collection unnecessary and lets a derived in-memory index do the job — strictly simpler, and it cannot drift or orphan the way a two-document transactional reservation can (precisely the failure the editor flagged). Destructive normalization removes a display-vs-match duality at no real cost for this population, and fail-closed is the only safe answer to an ambiguous authentication identity.

*Later updated by: N65 (2026-07-08 — the `pendingPriorEmail` alias the index was to carry is dropped: under Ghost-first-gated updates Book never commits an email Ghost has not accepted, so the index carries no alias).*

### D98 — Multi-store write ordering + compensation; dangling-ref scrub on delete; orphan detection folded into the reconciliation audit (no dedicated sweep)
Composite **R7** (headshot/delete/create span Firestore + GCS + Ghost with no ordering or compensation) and **R12** (delete leaves dangling `bigBrotherId`/`stars`). **Headshot:** write both GCS objects (512² + 96²) first and advance the Firestore `headshotVersion` pointer **last**, so the pointer never names objects that don't exist — a partial failure leaves the profile on its prior version (no broken image) and at worst orphaned new objects; builds on the opaque-token `headshotVersion` (R16) so there is no read-increment race. **Delete cascade:** delete the **Ghost member first** (synchronous, D96) and abort cleanly if it fails (Book untouched, admin retries); then the Book-side steps (GCS objects, `users` doc, profile doc), each idempotent so a re-run completes a partial delete. **Create** is Ghost-first (D96); the rare Ghost-created-then-Firestore-failed case leaves a cleanable Ghost orphan. **R12 dangling refs — scrub-and-proceed:** on delete, scan the in-memory dataset (free on the single authoritative instance — an in-memory filter, not a Firestore query) for inbound `bigBrotherId == id` and `stars` containing `id`, clear/remove them in the same operation, then delete; graceful rendering of any straggler is the backstop. **No dedicated periodic orphan-sweep job:** orphaned GCS objects fall under the D94 lifecycle rule, orphaned Ghost members under the existing D55 reconciliation audit, dangling refs under scrub-on-delete — so a sweep would duplicate existing tools. The **D55 reconciliation audit's scope is widened to also report Book-internal orphans** (a dangling `bigBrotherId`, a `users` doc with no live profile). Records to `API-SPEC §3`/`§6`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.1`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §7`/`§8`; extends the D55 audit.
**Why:** Synchronous pushes surface failures at the moment they happen, the single in-memory dataset makes inbound-reference scans free, and idempotent re-runnable steps make partial failures recoverable — so the failure modes shrink to rare, human-noticed, recoverable cases whose residue is already covered by D94 (GCS) and the D55 audit (Ghost + now Book-internal). Pointer-last and Ghost-first-abort-clean ensure each partial failure leaves a benign state, not a broken one; a dedicated sweep would add a scheduled job duplicating mechanisms that already exist.

*Later updated by: D115 (the Ghost-first member delete reused to revoke a de-brothered brother's Ghost access; a de-brothered profile is expected to have no Ghost member, exempted from the audit's `missingGhostMember` drift).*

### D99 — Observability for the write seam: trace IDs on the save path, and one consolidated periodic sysadmin job (reconciliation audit + login canary + contract test) *(Linear: OFC-181)*
Composite **R9** (no request/trace correlation across the multi-store save), **R10** (Ghost-push failure has no alert; the audit is manual, so drift accumulates silently), and **R14** (Ghost-contract brittleness; the account-redirect can silently revert to two-master). **R9:** propagate the Cloud Run-injected trace/correlation id (`X-Cloud-Trace-Context`) through every log line of a save, the Ghost call, and the audit entry — simplified by D96's synchronous model, since a save is now one request rather than an async span. **R10:** under D96, push failures surface to the user immediately, so the silent-divergence complaint is answered at the source; the remaining periodic drift-detection is handled by moving the D55 reconciliation audit from **manual-only to a scheduled run** that auto-detects Book↔Ghost drift (including a deceased brother whose unsubscribe push failed — Book `false` vs. Ghost still subscribed) and the Book-internal orphans added in D98, alerting on findings and on a failed run; the existing sign-in-denial-burst alert and the D83 cache-age/listener watchdog remain. **No standalone Ghost-push-failure-rate alert** (editor's call: sync surfaces individual failures and the scheduled audit catches drift). **R14:** document/pin the depended-on Ghost surfaces (member-session JWT/JWKS, the Portal redirect, the Admin-API shapes), and run the `IdentityProvider` contract test on a schedule (D72), not only at build. The dominant risk when the bridge breaks is confirmed to be **sign-in failure → Book unusable** (not two-master drift), detected by a scheduled **login canary** — valuable especially during quiet stretches, before a newsletter send puts the whole membership at the door at once. On the account-redirect: it was **verified that Ghost Portal pages cannot be disabled server-side**, so the only prevention is removing the profile-edit links from the theme; a member who types the obscure Portal profile URL can still edit name/email/newsletter in Ghost. This is accepted as a low/non-zero residual — the scheduled audit catches the resulting drift, and a Ghost-side email change is **self-surfacing** (the member is locked out of Book under the new address, since D96's prior-email alias covers only Book-initiated changes, prompting an admin contact often before the audit runs). No dedicated account-redirect canary (the login canary + audit + self-surfacing lockout cover it). **Consolidation:** the reconciliation audit, the login canary, and the scheduled contract test run as **one periodic sysadmin health-check job** (Cloud Scheduler → an admin endpoint — one wake-up under scale-to-zero), daily by default, reporting/alerting on what it finds. Amends D61 (trace ids; scheduled audit) and extends D55 (the audit is scheduled and also reports Book-internal orphans); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.1`/`§5.1`/`§2.1`, `PRD §6.1`.
**Why:** Synchronous push (D96) converts R10's silent divergence into loud, immediate failures, so what remains is periodic drift-detection and contract-monitoring — best served by *scheduling the reconciliation audit we already have* plus one login canary, consolidated into a single job to minimize scale-to-zero wake-ups, rather than building new per-failure alerting machinery. The verified inability to disable Ghost Portal makes the two-master path irreducible but low and self-surfacing, adequately covered without a dedicated canary; and the login canary is justified primarily by Book-availability, the bridge's true dominant failure.
