*Later updated by: D101 (post-restore reconciliation triggered immediately, not at the next scheduled run), D103 (the audit gains a scoped write for the newsletter flag), D106 (the before/after role-change audit feeds the forensic privileged-roster log), D115 (a de-brothered profile is exempted from `missingGhostMember` drift), D120 (the audit also emits an out-of-band email-bounce report each run).*

---

## Triage — Session 4 (4b — async ops, restore, DR & the read-architecture revisit) (2026-06-11)

The back half of Session 4 — the operations / restore / continuity cluster (composite **R4, R11, R15, R5, S13, R21**), the read-architecture / out-of-band-writer cache-coherence revisit, and the two carry-forwards from 4a (out-of-band-writer uniqueness enforcement; whether the reconciliation audit should actively honor a Ghost-side unsubscribe). The session opened by **re-examining the premise** and collapsed most of the cluster at its root: Book has no regular post-launch bulk-write need, so the online async-job machinery the pre-4a brief proposed (`TRIAGE-BRIEF-4.md` §5–§6) is not built at all — which dissolves the coherence fork and the out-of-band-uniqueness carry-forward together. Four decisions, **D100–D103**. Recording mechanics unchanged: the composite §12 worksheet + these D-numbers; the eight delivered docs are propagated once in the resolution pass.

### D100 — Book has no online bulk-write operations; restore is an offline maintenance event; whole-database work is external operator tooling (retires the async-job machinery)
The pre-4a brief and Session 0 framed the operations cluster around **online asynchronous jobs** (Cloud Run Jobs / Cloud Tasks) for backup, restore, bulk-CSV apply, bulk-delete, regenerate-all-thumbnails, and migration — with progress polling, resumable chunking, in-job uniqueness enforcement, and a maintenance-flag-*while-still-serving* mechanism to reconcile the single authoritative instance (D83) with an out-of-band writer. Re-examining the premise dissolved the need: **Book has no regular post-launch bulk-write workload.** An actuarial estimate puts expected deaths at ≈2/month, and with more than half of deaths never reported to MITAA, the realistic MITAA reconciliation is a handful of single manual edits per year — not a bulk import; new initiates are individual admin Add-Brother actions; nothing recurring writes many records at once online. Book therefore adopts a hard invariant: **every write into Book is a single authenticated edit through the one live instance — there is no online bulk-write path at all.** Consequences: **(1)** the **MITAA bulk-CSV import is deferred to the post-MVP backlog** (amends D52/D59/D68; added to `PRD §3.2`), pulled forward only if a real need emerges; **(2)** **restore becomes an offline maintenance event** (D101) rather than an online async job — Book goes hard-down, the database is replaced, and the instance cold-hydrates from the restored data on restart (D85), so there is no in-flight cache coherence, no concurrent second writer, and no snapshot-listener storm to manage; **(3)** **regenerate-all-thumbnails and schema migrations are operator / offline tooling**, not online Book features — migrations keep D71's numbered-idempotent-script discipline with R15's execution hardening folded in (run in-network as a Cloud Run Job/Task with cursor-resume, never from a laptop; backward-compatible-read-then-migrate or drain the old revision before migrating, closing the mid-deploy old-code race); **(4)** the **Directory bulk-delete is dropped from MVP** — single-record delete on the Profile page (D51) covers the admin error-correction case (D77); **(5)** any genuinely unforeseen bulk update is handled by taking Book offline, performing it, and returning to service. This **dissolves both out-of-band-writer carry-forwards**: with no online second writer, the read-architecture coherence fork D83 handed forward evaporates, and email-uniqueness (D97) has no out-of-band writer to enforce against except an offline restore, which re-validates uniqueness structurally on a down system (D101). Retires composite **R4** at the root; reduces **R11** to a feature-deferral and **R15** to an ops-discipline note. Records composite **R4** (a), **R11** (a, descoped), **R15** (a); supersedes the pre-4a brief's async-job direction (and its provisional D100); amends D41/D52/D59/D68/D71.
**Why:** The async-job apparatus existed for exactly one scenario — mutating the database in bulk *online* without disrupting live service — and that scenario does not occur in Book's actual operating profile. Building resumable jobs, in-job uniqueness, and serve-while-writing coherence to support an operation that never runs is mechanism for its own sake; removing it is the single largest simplification available in the whole triage, and it makes the system markedly easier to reason about, secure, and test (every write is one authenticated edit). Taking Book briefly offline for the rare destructive or bulk operation costs nothing for a bi-annual-traffic volunteer directory where no lives or revenue ride on uptime. Deferring the MITAA import is justified by measured cadence, not guesswork: at ≈1 reconcilable MITAA death per month a CSV pipeline is unjustified, and the feature is cheap to add later if the need ever materializes.

*Later updated by: D101 (the offline-restore event detailed), D105 (cited in the no-step-up rationale — restore offline, Directory bulk-delete dropped), D114 (the thumbnail-regenerate feature dropped entirely), D118 (the offline-restore "hard maintenance" state generalized into a first-class, script-triggered operator maintenance page).*

### D101 — Restore hardening: offline replacement, structural validation, verbatim role restore, a forensic privileged-roster log, and immediate post-restore Ghost reconciliation *(Linear: OFC-175)*
Restore — the suite's biggest multi-lens hotspot (composite **R5** + **S13**, with the **P3** facet already removed by D77 and the listener-storm facet by D83) — is hardened now that D100 makes it an **offline** operation. **The model:** Book enters hard maintenance (serves a "down for maintenance" response; the single instance neither writes nor serves its cache as authoritative), the three collections are replaced from the backup, and Book restarts and cold-hydrates its cache and in-memory email index (D85/D97) from the restored data. Because the system is down during the replace, the listener-storm/OOM, in-flight-coherence, and out-of-band-uniqueness concerns all vanish — there is no live instance to be a second writer. **Structural validation before the write (retained):** even though field-level *edit* rules stay intentionally bypassed (D63 — restore means "be exactly this snapshot," not "merge corrections"), the backup must pass structural validation before it is applied — big-brother **cycle detection**, **ID uniqueness**, **email uniqueness** (normalized; primary + `alternateEmail` in one namespace, per D97), and **reference integrity** — so a corrupt or tampered backup cannot reintroduce a structurally broken database; this is the one remaining home for uniqueness enforcement by a non-live writer. **Role restore is verbatim — no gating:** restore writes the complete backup including `role`. Gating roles was rejected (Forrest's call): it is theater against an actor who already holds admin or can mint it, and it would force an incoherent "be exactly this snapshot *except* roles, which come from the store you are restoring *because* it is untrustworthy" semantic. The residual "an admin is socially-engineered into restoring a tampered backup that plants `role: admin`" vector is one Book cannot close from the inside; it is pushed down to **backup-bucket security** (below) and named in the documented threat model (S5, Session 5). **Forensic privileged-roster log (retained, non-preventive):** because the Book↔Ghost reconciliation audit cannot see role changes (roles are Book-internal — Ghost has no role data to compare), a restore would otherwise alter who is an admin with no trace anywhere, so on restore Book writes an audit-stream (D61) entry recording the **resulting** privileged-account roster (always computable from the restored data), plus a **delta** against the prior state *when that prior state is readable*. It prevents nothing (consistent with the no-gating decision) but makes a planted or fat-fingered admin visible for later review at zero cost. **Immediate post-restore Ghost reconciliation:** a rollback can strand Ghost *ahead* of Book, so on restart the reconciliation audit (D99) is triggered **immediately** (not left to the next scheduled run), surfaces the Book↔Ghost discrepancy report, and the admin approves a bulk Book→Ghost re-push to re-align — **admin-reviewed, not automatic** (a blind mass re-push could itself mass re-subscribe/unsubscribe). This preserves D55's read-only-into-Book invariant (excepting the narrow D103 newsletter-flag case). **Backup-as-crown-jewels (S13 second half):** the backup bucket is **ACL-restricted** (service-account/admin only, never public) and relies on **GCS default encryption at rest** (CMEK judged overkill for a volunteer nonprofit), with retention pinned at **3 months** (aligned with D94/P16); the downloadable manual archive (D63) leaves Book's controls, so the USER-MANUAL names the admin as its custodian. **A signed manifest is skipped** for MVP (Forrest's call) — structural validation forecloses structural corruption and the no-gating posture already treats the role vector as a lower-level concern, so cryptographic input-authentication adds key-management overhead for little marginal protection (it remains the natural upgrade if backup *authentication* is ever wanted). Records composite **R5** (a) and **S13** (a); amends D63 (restore gains the offline model + structural validation + the forensic log).
**Why:** Restore is rare, destructive, and inherently a take-everything-down-and-replace event, so the offline model is the honest shape of the operation, not a compromise — and it deletes an entire class of online-coherence problems for free. Structural validation is the line between "restore a snapshot" and "restore a snapshot *and* any corruption an attacker put in it." Verbatim role restore follows from recognizing that in-Book role protection cannot defend against an actor who already has restore privilege, so the defense must live below Book; the forensic roster log keeps the consequence *visible* without pretending to prevent it. Admin-reviewed Ghost re-push respects that a rollback's mass divergence is exactly the situation where a human should see the discrepancy before a bulk push fires. Bucket ACL + encryption-at-rest are the right controls for an off-platform PII archive; CMEK and signed manifests are disproportionate to a volunteer nonprofit's threat model.

*Later updated by: D105 (the socially-engineered-restore residual absorbed into the documented threat model), D106 (the server-enforced role-change audit extends the forensic privileged-roster log).*

### D102 — DR / continuity posture: ≈24h RPO, explicit single-region, and continuous backup-integrity verification via ephemeral script-provisioned staging *(Linear: OFC-181)*
Disaster-recovery and continuity posture (composite **R21**), previously unstated, is pinned. **RPO ≈ 24 hours** from the daily automated backup (D63) — and given Book's write volume (≈2 edits/month) the *effective* data loss on a restore is usually zero. **Single-region is accepted explicitly:** Cloud Run / Firestore / GCS are regional, and multi-region cost and complexity are unjustified for a bi-annual-traffic volunteer directory; the region-failure **RTO is on the order of hours** (redeploy + restore), bounded in practice less by the sub-hour restore itself than by admin-awareness-and-priority latency (no lives or revenue ride on uptime). **Continuous backup-integrity verification** is done via **ephemeral, script-provisioned staging:** rather than maintaining a standing staging environment, Book ships **environment setup/teardown scripts** (infrastructure-as-code) that stand a throwaway environment up on demand; a periodic job (weekly by default, tunable) uses them to spin up a fresh environment, restore the latest backup into it, run structural validation (D101) + a hydrate-and-count smoke check + checksums, then tear it all down — nothing idle, nothing to rot between runs. This does **double duty:** those same setup/teardown scripts *are* the single-region DR recovery runbook, so exercising them on every backup test continuously rehearses "stand Book up from nothing," de-risking the RTO. The scripts join the sysadmin tooling inventory alongside the migration scripts (D71) and the pre-launch loaders (D57); the backup-verification job runs separately from D99's daily production health-check. Build-time implementation note: Firestore is the one resource heavier to create/destroy than the rest, so the lightest viable form is a persistent-but-*empty* staging project (near-zero idle cost) into which the scripts provision an ephemeral Firestore database + bucket per test. Amends D72 (staging becomes on-demand / ephemeral rather than a standing environment) and D63 (adds continuous integrity verification); records composite **R21** (a).
**Why:** Stating RPO/RTO honestly (24h, hours) matches the application's actual stakes rather than importing enterprise targets. Ephemeral, script-driven staging resolves the legitimate worry about maintaining an environment forever — there is nothing to maintain between uses — while turning the backup test from a standing cost into the very thing that keeps the DR procedure exercised and trustworthy. A silently-corrupt backup discovered only at restore time, under duress, is the failure R21 names; periodic test-restore-and-checksum is the standard cure, and folding it into provisioning scripts that double as the recovery runbook is strictly better than a one-off staging round-trip.

### D103 — The newsletter-subscription flag reconciles bidirectionally by most-recent-change-wins (a scoped, timestamp-based exception to D55's read-only-into-Book invariant)
The newsletter-subscription consent flag (`allowNewsletterEmail`) is the one field with a legitimate *two-way* path: Book can change it (the brother edits his profile, or mark-deceased forces it off per D55/D80) and **Ghost can change it** (the brother clicks "unsubscribe" in a PBE News email, which Ghost records as a member activity). D96 deferred to 4b whether the reconciliation audit should actively honor a Ghost-side unsubscribe (write Book) or merely report it. The obstacle was that the audit, seeing only that Book and Ghost disagree, could not tell **which side changed** — and resolving by value in the wrong direction would re-introduce the exact "failed-push-undo" hazard D55's read-only invariant exists to prevent (Book opted out, the push failed, and a naive reconcile re-subscribes him). The resolution (Forrest's) supplies the missing causality with **timestamps:** Ghost logs subscribe/unsubscribe events with a time, and Book records a `newsletterConsentChangedAt` on every change to the flag (including the mark-deceased force and the D80 un-decease restore). The reconciliation audit (D99) then resolves drift on this one field by **most-recent-change-wins** — the later timestamp is the brother's final intent — and **applies the correction itself**, logging each resolution: if **Book** changed later (e.g. a failed opt-out push), it **re-pushes Book→Ghost** (the always-allowed direction — which also self-heals the deceased-still-subscribed harm of R1b/R10); if **Ghost** changed later (a Ghost-side unsubscribe), it **writes Ghost→Book**. The Ghost→Book write is a **deliberate, narrowly-scoped exception to D55**, safe precisely because the direction is chosen by authoritative timestamp rather than by blind value — the audit stays strictly read-only-into-Book for **every other field** (email, name, etc. remain report-only). "Book never changed it" maps cleanly to "Ghost wins" (unknown Book-time = oldest), so the only indeterminate case is two changes at the same instant across two systems, which does not occur in practice (real changes are months apart; both platforms are NTP-sane); were it ever to occur, the audit falls back to report-for-human. Every auto-correction is logged, and an alert on anomalous correction *volume* is the tripwire for a bug or a misbehaving Ghost timestamp (and any wrong flip is reversible by the brother re-toggling). **Two build-time dependencies:** Book gains the `newsletterConsentChangedAt` field (schema addition), and the scheme depends on Ghost's member-activity API exposing the subscribe/unsubscribe **timestamp programmatically** (not UI-only) — a build-time must-verify, with the endpoint added to D99's list of pinned, canaried Ghost surfaces (R14's category). Amends D55 (the scoped bidirectional exception for one field) and supersedes D96's "the stale Book record persists until the brother re-enables it in Book" with "the audit reconciles it automatically by most-recent-wins"; amends D99 (the audit gains scoped write for this field); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.1`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §3.1`/`§8`, `PRD §6.1`.
**Why:** Honoring a brother's unsubscribe is the consent-respecting outcome, and doing it automatically is *more* protective than waiting for an admin to notice a report — it only required a way to do it without ever auto-*undoing* an opt-out. Timestamps are exactly that: they convert D55's "the audit can't know the right answer" into "the audit knows the right answer," removing the reason the read-only rule existed *for this field* while leaving it fully in force everywhere else. The exception is principled and bounded — one field, resolution by authoritative time, every action logged — and it folds the deceased-still-emailed failure mode into the same self-healing mechanism as a bonus.

---

## Triage — Session 5 (Auth & input hardening) (2026-06-11)

Brief: [`history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-5.md`](history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-5.md). The auth-seam and untrusted-input cluster — composite **S3, S4, S5, S7, S8, S11, S14, S15, S16, S18, S19** — plus the implementation home of **P9**'s `Referrer-Policy` (ratified (a) in Session 3) inside the S14 headers work. The session's character was the high ratify ratio the plan predicted: the security *architecture* was credited by both reviewers, and these eleven are gaps where the spec stated an *intent* ("out-of-scope fields are rejected," "the JWT is verified," "the last admin can't be demoted") without pinning the *enforcement mechanism* — so all eleven are **(a) fix** (S5 mixed (a)+(b)), the judgment being *which mechanism / how deep*, not *whether*. The one genuine fork (S5 step-up) was narrowed by Session 4: D99 closed the email-collision takeover path (S2), and D100/D101 took **restore offline** and **dropped Directory bulk-delete**, shrinking the online destructive surface to role grants + single delete. Five decisions, **D104–D108**. Recording mechanics unchanged: the composite §12 worksheet + these D-numbers; the eight delivered docs are propagated once in the resolution pass.

### D104 — Auth-token hardening: pin the JWT algorithm, and bind the callback with a single-use nonce + a redirect allowlist
The Ghost member-JWT verification (D20/§2.1 — "signature, `aud`, `iss`, `exp`" against Ghost's JWKS) is hardened on two axes. **Algorithm pinning (composite S7):** verification **pins Ghost's specific asymmetric `alg` and `kid`** from JWKS and **rejects `alg:none` and every symmetric algorithm** outright — foreclosing the two classic forges (an unsigned `none` token, and an `HS256` token that HMACs with the RSA *public* key, which is by definition public); the forged-`alg` cases join the §6.6 JWT-against-mocked-JWKS tests. **Callback binding (composite S8):** the auth callback carries a **single-use, Book-generated `state`/nonce**, stored server-side at flow initiation and verified at the callback, so an attacker cannot deliver `…/auth/callback#token=<attacker token>` and silently establish the victim's browser as a session for the *attacker's* identity (forced login → data-poisoning/phishing); and **every redirect target** in the bridge (Ghost Portal's `return`, the callback URL) is **hardcoded/allowlisted** so neither is caller-parameterizable (closing the open-redirect path that would leak the `#token` to an attacker origin). The nonce is layered **with** D20's fragment-carried token, not in place of it: the two are orthogonal — D20 keeps the token out of logs/history/`Referer` (anti-*leakage*), the nonce ties the flow to a Book-initiated request (anti-*forgery*) — so they compose. Records composite **S7** (a), **S8** (a); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.1`/`§2.7`, `API-SPEC §2`.
**Why:** Both are standard JWT/OAuth-callback hardening of controls that already exist in intent — pure defense at the auth boundary with no design tension. Pinning the algorithm is the single cheapest way to foreclose the most common JWT-verification bypass; the nonce + redirect allowlist are the matching pair that turn the fragment handoff (already leak-resistant by D20) into a forge-resistant one as well.

*Later updated by: D105 (layered with the nonce + alg-pin to close the replay / forced-login vectors as part of the Ghost-SPOF posture).*

### D105 — Ghost is an accepted single point of total compromise: close the replay/forced-login vectors, document the blast-radius dependency in a threat model, and add no step-up authentication *(Linear: OFC-181)*
Book authenticates with the token from Ghost's `/members/api/session`, whose `aud`/`iss` are the *Ghost members API*, not Book — so the token is a bearer credential not minted *for* Book, and Book derives *all* authorization (including admin) from the resolved email (composite **S5**, building on the accepted dependency D54). The finding's three facets are resolved by type:
- **Replay / forced-login (closable → closed):** D104's nonce binds the handoff to a Book-initiated flow, D104's alg-pin forecloses forged tokens, and D22's 4-hour server-side session cap bounds how long a stolen token stays useful. The residual — an attacker who steals a *live* member token via XSS or a malicious element on the shared `pbe400.org` origin — is closed by **hardening `pbe400.org`**, which is named as an **explicit dependency in the threat model**. A Book-specific-audience token would be ideal but Ghost Pro exposes no way to mint one (D20 documents exactly this constraint), so handoff-binding is the realistic mechanism.
- **Blast radius (accepted → documented):** that anyone able to obtain a member's — or an admin's — Ghost token thereby obtains the corresponding Book access is an **architectural given of D54** (Ghost *is* the authentication; there is nothing below it to appeal to). This is recorded **(b) as-designed** with a written threat-model note: "Book's authorization trust equals Ghost Pro's account security."
- **Step-up (rejected):** **no step-up authentication is added.** Session 4 shrank the online destructive surface — D99 closed the email-collision *takeover* path (S2), and D100/D101 made **restore an offline operator event** and **dropped Directory bulk-delete** — leaving only **role grants (D51) and single-profile delete** as online destructive actions, both already gated by a typed-acknowledgment confirmation (D51/D52) and audited with before/after (S18) and surfaced in the forensic privileged-roster log (D101). Decisively, **an *in-Book* step-up authenticates nothing new**, because every factor Book possesses derives from the same Ghost identity — only an *out-of-band* channel (email/SMS to a separately-controlled address) would be meaningful, and that is infrastructure and friction unjustified for a 1–2-admin volunteer directory whose remaining destructive actions are already confirmed, reversible, and audited (Forrest's call: scale and consequences don't warrant it). This threat model also absorbs D101's residual (the socially-engineered-restore-plants-admin vector pushed down here from the no-gating restore decision). Records composite **S5** (a)+(b); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.1`/`§2.2` + a new threat-model note; cites D22, D54, D99, D100, D101.
**Why:** The dependency on Ghost is real, deliberate (D54), and unremovable, so the honest posture is to close every vector that *can* be closed (replay, forced login, redirect) and then *document* the irreducible remainder rather than dress it in security theater. Step-up is exactly such theater here: an attacker who holds a valid Ghost-derived session would satisfy any in-Book re-confirmation with that same session, so the only meaningful second factor is out-of-band — disproportionate when the protected actions are already confirmed, audited, reversible, and few, and when the population is 1–2 administrators. Detect-and-reverse (the S18 audit + D101 forensic log) is the right-sized control at this scale; prevent-with-a-second-factor is not.

### D106 — Server-side authorization model: a positive per-role write-field allowlist, an explicit object-level predicate, and a server-enforced last-admin invariant
The "who may write what, to whose record" contract is pinned across three findings that share one home (composite **S3**, **S4**, **S18**). **Field-level — positive allowlist (S3):** writes are governed by a **per-role writable-field allowlist**; any field outside it is **rejected (422/403), never silently ignored**. **All** system/verification/Ghost fields (`id`, `role`, `lastVerifiedDate`, `verifiedBy`, `lastModified`, `headshotVersion`, `adminNote`, `ghostMemberId`) are unwritable through PATCH/POST by *every* role — they are set only by dedicated server actions (the server-set `verify`, D28; the role toggle, D51; the D96 `ghostMemberId` capture) — and consent/privacy fields are **owner-only** (a manager editing another brother cannot write them). The enumerated write matrix lives **alongside the read-projection capability matrix** (C6's home) as its **write-side dual** — the same per-role table applied to writes — so it extends the credited server-side projection rather than introducing a second enforcement mechanism. **Object-level — explicit predicate (S4):** the server enforces **`request.profileId == session.profileId OR session.role ∈ {manager, admin}`** before any PATCH/PUT (Constitution IDs are contiguous guessable integers, so a bare "is authenticated" check is an IDOR); **owner-vs-other is enforced on `verify`** (a plain brother stamps provenance on no one but, where allowed, himself — PRD §4.3 reserves verifying *others* to manager/admin); and **stars writes are scoped to the `stars` field exclusively**, never coercible into a `role` write on the shared `users` doc. Field-level and object-level are the two orthogonal axes of the same endpoint, decided together. **The invariant — last admin (S18):** the **last-admin safeguard is enforced server-side** in `PUT /api/users/{id}/role` (reject demotion of the only remaining admin), independent of the UI where D51 had described it — a direct API call must not be able to remove the final admin and lock the org out of backup/restore, add/delete, role changes, and Ghost sync; **every role change is audit-logged with before/after**, which also feeds D101's forensic privileged-roster log and the S5 detect-and-reverse posture. Records composite **S3** (a), **S4** (a), **S18** (a); records to `API-SPEC §3`/`§4`/`§5`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §6.1`/`§8`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.4`, `PRD §5.7.10`; the role audit extends D101's log.
**Why:** The reviewers' point was never that the design intends the wrong thing — it intends rejection, a server-set `verify`, and a last-admin guard — but that the *enforcement mechanism* was unpinned, and a faithful-but-naïve build of "reject out-of-scope fields" as a denylist or an ignore-unknown-fields handler would silently open mass-assignment, IDOR, and a UI-only invariant into real escalation paths. A positive allowlist, an explicit object-level predicate, and a server-side invariant are the standard closures, and framing the write allowlist as the *dual of the read projection* keeps Book's single-enforcement-point strength intact rather than bolting on a parallel system.

### D107 — Input and HTTP-boundary hardening: decoded-image bounding, a strict URL-scheme allowlist, a strict CSP with the standard headers (landing P9's Referrer-Policy), and CORS + host-only-cookie scoping
The untrusted-input and response/request-boundary defenses Book lacked beyond HSTS, bundled (composite **S11**, **S15**, **S14**/**P9**, **S19**). **Image pipeline (S11):** uploads cap **decoded width×height and total pixels before/at decode** — rejecting **above ~40 MP**, a threshold far above any real headshot so a legitimate large photo is never bounced back to the user to shrink in an external editor (a byte-size cap alone does *not* bound decoded dimensions, leaving a decompression-bomb DoS) — plus **decoder memory/time limits**, a **magic-byte check** (not the declared `Content-Type` alone), and a **pinned, patched, least-privileged** imaging library (sharp/libvips-class transcoders are a recurring RCE/DoS surface). **URL scheme (S15):** `links[].url`, `obituaryUrl`, and `inMemoriamUrl` are validated with a **strict `http`/`https` scheme allowlist on write** (reject `javascript:`/`data:`/all others), and rendered as anchors with **`rel="noopener noreferrer"`**, never interpolated into a dangerous sink — closing stored-XSS-on-click. **Security headers (S14, landing P9):** a **strict CSP** (`script-src`/`style-src`/`connect-src`/`img-src`/`frame-ancestors`) with **no `unsafe-inline`**, which **must explicitly allowlist D62's Mixpanel script and connect origin** plus the Ghost/CDN image origins (so the strict policy does not silently break analytics); `X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff`; a framing policy (`frame-ancestors`/`X-Frame-Options`); and **P9's `Referrer-Policy`** (`strict-origin-when-cross-origin` or stricter, so the Name-Search term and `/brother/:id` path do not leak as `Referer` to `/api`, `/img`, or external obituary links) — all documented alongside HSTS in §6.4. **Transport scoping (S19):** **deny-by-default CORS on `/api/*`** (no credentialed `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` reflection, especially for `pbe400.org`), and the **session cookie host-only** on `book.pbe400.org` (no `Domain=.pbe400.org`, or Ghost/any sibling subdomain could read it) — extending the host-only discipline already stated for the CDN cookie (D23) to the session cookie. Records composite **S11** (a), **S15** (a), **S14** (a), **S19** (a) and lands **P9**'s implementation; amends **D64** (the headers list grows from HSTS-only to CSP + nosniff + framing + Referrer-Policy); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.5`/`§2.7`/`§6.4`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §7`/`§8`, `API-SPEC §6`, `PRD §5.7.4`/`§5.7.5`/`§5.7.7`.
**Why:** These are the standard boundary defenses for an app that stores and renders user-supplied content (names, links, `adminNote`, obituary URLs) *and* loads a third-party analytics script — exactly the profile where a CSP, a decoded-dimension bound, and a strict scheme allowlist each close a concrete class of attack the spec left open. The 40 MP image cap is set high deliberately: the goal is to stop decompression bombs without ever degrading the upload UX for a brother with a genuine high-resolution photo. The one content-decision — allowlisting Mixpanel in the CSP — is what lets a *strict* policy coexist with D62's analytics instead of forcing `unsafe-inline` or a loose policy.

*Later updated by: D126 (the image origin is `'self'` in the CSP `img-src` — images are app-served from the same origin; with no Cloud CDN cookie, the host-only-cookie discipline this decision extended now centers on the session cookie).*

### D108 — `DevIdentityProvider` is locked out of production by four independent layers, not one env gate
The dev identity provider (D72) issues a Ghost-free session for any chosen identity and role, guarded today by a **single** env check ("the backend refuses to start if the dev provider is combined with a production configuration"). Because a single misconfiguration there would let anyone mint any identity at any role — including admin — against production (composite **S16**), the control is hardened with **four independent layers**: **(1)** the dev provider is **compiled out of the production bundle entirely** (a separate entry point / build-time exclusion), so the prod artifact does not *contain* its code and cannot instantiate it even if mis-configured — the load-bearing layer, since "cannot be loaded" strictly dominates "is disabled"; **(2)** D72's **runtime env gate is retained** as a second, independent check; **(3)** a **CI/build assertion** fails the build if the production artifact can instantiate the dev provider; **(4)** a **startup alert** fires if the dev provider is ever loaded under a prod-like configuration. This hardens a deliberate, valuable test seam (D21/D72) without removing it, and aligns with D102's move to ephemeral, script-provisioned staging — throwaway environments stood up on demand are now the dev provider's only legitimate home, never anything adjacent to prod. Records composite **S16** (a); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.6` (hardens D72) and `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (the CI gate).
**Why:** The failure mode is the worst the system has — total authentication bypass against production — while the defending layers are nearly free, so defense-in-depth is plainly worth it; in particular, compiling the provider out of the prod bundle converts the risk from "one config flag stands between prod and total bypass" to "the bypass code is not present to run," which is the qualitatively stronger guarantee. The remaining three layers are cheap belt-and-suspenders that make a regression loud (CI) and observable (startup alert) rather than silent.

---

## Triage — Session 6 (UI / a11y + leftovers) (2026-06-11)

Brief: [`history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-6.md`](history/TRIAGE-BRIEF-6.md). The final triage sitting — the UI/accessibility + leftovers cluster, composite **U2, U3, U4, U5, U7, C9, C15, R18**. The small, high-ratify tail the plan predicted: one genuine values fork (**U7**, the privacy-toggle nudge vs. mobile calm), one real design gap needing a UX contract (**U3/R18**, session-expiry recovery), one already-resolved item confirmed (**U4**, closed in Session 3 by **D95**), and four clean ratifications. A clean sweep — every finding dispositioned **(a)** (U7 taking the inline-active-side middle path; C15 documentation-only with no D-number). Accessibility's hard-policy status (D32/D67) gave U2/U5/U7 weight beyond their `should-fix` severities. Four new decisions, **D109–D113** (U4 stays D95; C15 takes no number). Recording mechanics unchanged: the composite §12 worksheet + these D-numbers; the eight delivered docs are propagated once in the resolution pass. **This closes triage** — next is the resolution / propagation pass (TRIAGE-PLAN §6): finalize `D77+`, propagate into the delivered docs, close **C4**'s remaining TBD mechanics, and fill the composite §13 summary.

### D109 — Session-expiry recovery: a mid-edit `401` (and mid-session image `403`) is handled non-destructively, re-authing without destroying the in-progress form
Book's session cookie carries an **absolute** 4-hour server-side cap (D22) — not a sliding idle timeout — so a genuinely long edit *will* hit the wall mid-Save, and that case cannot be prevented by silent renewal; a non-destructive recovery is therefore mandatory (composite **U3**). D25/§2.6 already preserve unsaved edits on a **412** (concurrency conflict); this extends the same "never lose the user's work" instinct to the previously-unspecified **401** (expired-session) path, which a naïve `window.location` re-bounce (D22) would have discarded. **The contract:** on a `401` from a state-changing request, **do not follow a redirect mid-XHR**; **preserve the in-progress form** exactly as left; surface a non-destructive "your session expired — sign in to save" affordance; on successful re-auth, **resume/re-submit the same Save carrying its original `If-Match`** (so a real concurrency conflict still reconciles via D25). **The mechanism (Forrest's call):** re-auth happens in a **child window**, not by navigating the editor tab — the `401` fires inside the user's Save click, so a popup to the Ghost bridge is permitted; the child completes the silent re-bounce and hands the new session back (`postMessage` / the shared `book.pbe400.org` cookie) and closes, leaving the editor tab and its in-memory form untouched with **zero PII written to disk** (a hidden-iframe variant is foreclosed by D107's `frame-ancestors` — the Ghost portal won't frame — so it is a child *window*). A **`sessionStorage` draft stash** is the **popup-blocked fallback only** (tab-scoped, cleared on tab close — a closer analog to D95's in-memory-while-open posture than `localStorage`, and the one bounded softening of "nothing on disk," flagged as such). **R18 folds in here:** the same cap lapse surfaces on image reads as a CDN signed-cookie `403` (D23, cookie TTL = session); on an image `403` the SPA triggers the **same** re-auth and **retries the image load** (the reissued cookie rides the new session) rather than rendering a broken image — one cap-lapse, one re-auth, both the pending Save and the failed image fetches resume. The reviewer's "reissue the CDN cookie ahead of expiry" does not apply: the cookie's life is pinned to the absolute-capped session, so there is nothing to reissue into past the 4-hour wall. Records composite **U3** (a), **R18** (a); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.3`/`§2.5`/`§2.6`, `API-SPEC §1.2`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §7`; cites D22, D23, D25, D95, D107.
**Why:** Losing a half-finished edit to a clock-driven session expiry is precisely the failure the 412 design went out of its way to prevent, so the 401 path must match that standard, not contradict it. The child-window handoff is the one mechanism that satisfies both load-bearing constraints at once — preserve the in-memory form *and* write no PII to disk — because the editor tab never navigates; the `sessionStorage` fallback is confined to the rare popup-blocked case and chosen (tab-scoped) to stay as close to D95 as a cross-redirect stash can. Treating the image `403` as the same event avoids inventing a second mechanism for what is physically one expiry.

*Later updated by: D126 (a cap-lapse on an image read now surfaces as a `401`/`403` from the app, not a Cloud CDN signed-cookie `403`; the same re-auth-and-retry applies).*

*Later updated by: D125 (the session record and login nonce persisted in Firestore so a scale-to-zero cold start no longer invalidates the session — returning the `401` to the rare absolute-cap event this recovery was designed for, rather than a routine one).*

*Later updated by: N98 (5.5j — the full recovery is built, and two of this decision's specifics are amended (Forrest's calls): the R18 image path is a lightweight re-arm-after-re-auth rather than status-aware per-image re-auth (an `<img>` can't read status), and the `sessionStorage` draft stash is dropped — the child-window design keeps the form in memory, so the stash would guard only a manual reload at a disk-PII cost; the blocked-popup fallback is instead in-memory + a recover-here message).*

### D110 — Name-search indexing runs in a Web Worker; D35's IndexedDB memo is dropped to keep no name-derived PII on disk *(Linear: OFC-180)*
D35 computes Double-Metaphone/Beider-Morse codes for ~2,000 profiles × multiple name fields and builds the Fuse.js index **at load on the main thread**; D74 deferred their *download* but nothing moved the *computation* off the main thread, so SPA init would jank — worst for exactly the older-hardware/slow-connection cohort the rest of the design optimizes for (composite **U5**). The index build + talisman phonetic-code generation move into a **Web Worker**: the directory grid renders immediately with **exact/substring matching available on the main thread**, and **fuzzy + phonetic search switches on when the worker posts "index ready"** (progressive enhancement — the grid is never blocked; the dataset reaches the worker by a `postMessage` structured clone of the already-in-memory profiles). **D35's IndexedDB memo escape hatch is dropped:** persisting name-keyed phonetic codes is **on-disk PII residue**, re-introducing exactly the shared-machine exposure **D95** eliminated for `/api/profiles` — so the codes are **recomputed in the worker each load** (cheap off the main thread) rather than memoized. (If load cost ever proves a real problem on the slowest hardware, a memo of *non-PII* derived artifacts could be revisited — but never name-keyed codes.) Records composite **U5** (a); amends **D35** (computation moves to a worker; IndexedDB memo removed); records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN` (client architecture) and `PRD §5.6.3`.
**Why:** A Web Worker is the standard fix for a CPU-bound load-time task that would otherwise freeze the UI, and progressive enhancement (exact search now, fuzzy/phonetic when ready) means the worker's latency is invisible. Dropping the IndexedDB memo keeps the no-disk-PII posture consistent end-to-end: it would be incoherent to serve `/api/profiles` `no-store` to keep names off disk (D95) while writing the same names' phonetic codes into IndexedDB.

### D111 — In-page help is delivered in two layers split along the WCAG line: AA-baseline labels/instructions ship with each page, enrichment stays Phase 6
`CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` ships the Directory/Profile/Admin pages in Phases 3–5 behind per-phase gates that include "a11y checks pass on the page," while **all** help wiring (`aria-describedby` helper text, persistent labels, the Radix-Popover `?` toggle-tips, and the manual assembly) is parked in **Phase 6** — a hidden inter-phase dependency, since AA-baseline help is load-bearing for pages whose a11y gates assert conformance (composite **U2**, confirmed against the plan). Help is split by its actual conformance level: the **AA-baseline layer — persistent labels and any *instructions a control needs to be usable* (WCAG 3.3.2 Labels or Instructions, plus label-in-name / programmatic association) — ships *with* each page in its own phase** (3–5), so those a11y gates are honest; the **above-baseline enrichment — the richer `?` toggle-tip explanations (≈ 3.3.5 Help, an AAA criterion) and the assembled `USER-MANUAL`** — stays in **Phase 6**, consistent with the project targeting **AA** (D67/D79), not AAA. D53's single help-content source is unchanged (authored once, can't drift); only the *wiring schedule* and the *per-phase gate wording* are refined (the early gates explicitly cover baseline labels/instructions, not the Phase-6 enrichment). Records composite **U2** (a); records to `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN §7`/`§10` and the phase gates; clarifies D53/D67.
**Why:** The dependency dissolves cleanly when help is split along the line WCAG already draws — AA labels/instructions are part of the conformance the early gates claim, so they must ship with the page; the toggle-tip enrichment is an AAA-level nicety the project doesn't gate on, so it can stay batched with the manual. This keeps the per-phase a11y gates truthful without pulling the whole help-and-manual workstream forward, and without touching D53's drift-proof single source.

### D112 — API contract evolution: a versioned `/api/roster` for the Linter and a stale-SPA refresh prompt, lightweight by design
There is no API versioning or deprecation policy (composite **C9**), exposing two facets of one missing capability. **The Linter contract:** the cross-language, independently-deployed Linter (D58) consumes `/api/roster`, so any shape change breaks it silently — `/api/roster` gains an **explicit contract version** (a `contractVersion` field or response header) plus a **minimal deprecation note** ("bump on breaking change; the Linter pins a version"), closing the silent-break. **The stale-SPA tab:** D73's content-hashed assets + no-cache HTML make a *fresh* load current but cannot force an *already-open* background tab to update across a deploy/migration — so the server **advertises the current client/contract version** (a header on `/api/*` or a tiny `/api/version`), the SPA compares it to its own build hash and, if stale, shows a **non-blocking "a new version is available — refresh" overlay** and **fails knowingly-stale writes gracefully** rather than sending malformed data. Deliberately **lightweight — no full versioning regime** for a 1–2-admin internal tool. Records composite **C9** (a); records to `API-SPEC` (the roster contract + version), `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.2`/`§6.5`; builds on D58 (Linter) and D73 (asset immutability).
**Why:** The two consumers that can desync from the live contract — a different-language batch client and a long-open browser tab — are exactly the ones D73's deploy story doesn't cover, and each is closed by a cheap, standard mechanism (a version token + a deprecation note for the Linter; a version check + refresh prompt for the SPA). A heavier scheme (negotiated versions, parallel contract support) would be over-built for a tool with one or two operators and a single first-party batch consumer.

### D113 — Privacy-toggle copy: show the currently-true consequence inline, house the opposite-state consequence in the help tip (presentation-only amendment of D45)
D45 renders every privacy/consent switch stating the concrete consequence of **both** sides in plain text; with the set now at seven (four share toggles + three consent flags, post-D56/D93), stacking seven *both-sentences* switches on a phone is real scrolling-fatigue against the design's own "calm resting interface" goal (composite **U7**). The middle path is taken: each switch shows **only the currently-true consequence inline in plain language** ("Brothers can reach you by email"), and the **opposite-state consequence moves into the existing D53 `?` toggle-tip** — roughly halving the at-rest text per toggle and cutting the mobile scroll, while **each control is still named in plain language** so flipping it remains an informed, considered act. This deliberately **preserves D45's core**: the label still says *exactly what this toggle controls* (not a terse "Share email" whose effect is hidden), so D45's cure for the MITAA failure mode — a single ambiguous "no emails" opt-out misread by a third of brothers — is intact; what changes is only that the *counterfactual* sentence is one tap away rather than always stacked. The seven get light grouping/disclosure so they don't read as an undifferentiated wall. **Presentation only** — the stored booleans, their `true` defaults, and the open-by-default nudge are unchanged. Records composite **U7** (a); **amends D45** (presentation); records to `PRD §5.7.3` and `ENGINEERING-DESIGN` (Profile UI); reuses D53's toggle-tip.
**Why:** The reviewer's clutter complaint is legitimate and the fix is component-compatible (D53 already provides the popover), but collapsing all the way to a terse label + tooltip would resurrect the hidden-consequence pattern D45 exists to prevent. Showing the active-side consequence inline is the smallest move that answers the mobile-calm goal without sacrificing informed consent: the user always sees, in plain words, what the toggle currently does, and a brother considering an opt-out still reads a plain consequence — the both-sides framing is merely demoted to the help tip rather than removed. Forrest's call, weighing D45's anti-dark-pattern intent against the calm-UI goal for an elderly, mobile, slow-connection audience.

---

## Resolution pass — additional decision (2026-06-11)

### D114 — The thumbnail "regenerate" feature is dropped entirely; re-derivation becomes a latent operator script (amends D100)
D100 moved whole-database "regenerate all thumbnails" to offline operator tooling while a per-id `POST /api/profiles/{id}/thumbnail:regenerate` endpoint and a Directory selection-scoped action still remained. Examined in the resolution pass, the feature's original motivation — that thumbnails might drift out of sync with their source headshots — is **structurally foreclosed by D98's pointer-last write ordering**: the 96² thumbnail and the 512² headshot are written together *before* the `headshotVersion` pointer advances, so a version's two objects are always a matched pair and no runtime drift can arise. The only remaining triggers are **developer events** — a thumbnail-spec change (e.g. dimensions/encoder settings) or a generation-bug fix — which are rare, code-initiated, and already covered by the offline migration-script discipline (D71/D100): the 512² headshots are always retained, so re-deriving every thumbnail is a small ad-hoc script written **if and when** a spec actually changes. Book therefore **drops the regenerate feature entirely** — no online button (Admin or Directory), no per-id endpoint, no dedicated test, no confirmation UX — while keeping the *capability* latent (re-derive from the retained headshots). A brother who wants a different thumbnail re-uploads/re-crops (the normal path). Amends **D100** (a step past "regenerate-all → operator tooling" to "no feature at all"); removes the `POST /api/profiles/{id}/thumbnail:regenerate` endpoint from `API-SPEC §6`; updates `DATABASE-SCHEMA §7`, `PRD §4.1`/`§5.6.1`/`§5.6.8`/`§5.7.5`/`§5.8.4`/`§5.8.5`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.6`, `USER-MANUAL`, and `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Session 5a).
**Why:** D100's mandate is to not build mechanism for operations that do not occur in Book's actual operating profile; a permanent regenerate feature is exactly that — built for a one-time hypothetical (a spec change or a bug fix) that a developer would handle with a script written at the time. D98's ordering removes the only steady-state drift the feature was meant to repair, so it defends against nothing that can happen at runtime, and the capability survives for free because the source headshots are retained. Removing it shrinks the surface (an endpoint, two UI affordances, a confirmation flow, a test, and the "skip deceased" footgun) at zero functional cost. Forrest's call.

---

## Post-resolution amendments (2026-06-12)

After the resolution / propagation pass closed (D77–D114), Forrest brought a short backlog of small design modifications kept since the design phase — none requiring architectural rework. Each was discussed and its forks resolved with Forrest before integration; the four genuine forks (the de-brother Ghost-revocation mechanism, de-brother reversibility, the maintenance-page mechanism, and the report-a-bug delivery channel) were decided directly. These seven decisions, **D115–D121**, are propagated into the eight delivered docs under the same single-touch-per-section discipline as the resolution pass. Source: [`history/amendments-to-Initial-book-design.md`](history/amendments-to-Initial-book-design.md).

### D115 — De-brothering: an admin-only flag that hides the record from brothers, deletes the Ghost member, denies Book sign-in, and is reversible by snapshot
PBE can, in rare and extreme circumstances, **de-brother** a member. Book models this as a new admin-only state `debrothered.isDebrothered` (with a mark-time snapshot, mirroring `DeceasedInfo`), **distinct from `deceased`**. Setting it has four coordinated effects. **(1) Read projection — hidden from brothers, flagged for staff:** a de-brothered record is **omitted entirely** from the brother-role projection (it disappears from the bulk download, search, filters, export, and a direct `/brother/{id}` hit returns `404`/`403` for a brother), while **managers and admins still see it**, rendered with a **strikethrough on the name** so the status is unmistakable while the record stays readable for staff. This is a new branch of the server-side projection (D5/D16/D19/D82), not a client filter. **(2) Block Book sign-in:** because Book derives identity from the resolved Ghost-JWT email (D96), `POST /api/auth/session` **denies a session when the resolved profile is de-brothered** — fully under Book's control, independent of Ghost. **(3) Revoke Ghost access + email via the existing delete path:** rather than a fake-email neutralization (Option 2 of the amendment — which would collide with the one-namespace email-uniqueness index (D97) and the email-as-identity model (D96), destroy the real email, and still need a separate force-unsubscribe), de-brothering reuses the **Ghost-first member delete** of D98 — removing the Ghost member blocks Ghost login and stops all newsletter/comment mail in one move — while the **Book profile is retained** (staff-visible, struck through). The reconciliation audit (D99) is taught one exception: a profile with `isDebrothered = true` is *expected* to have no Ghost member, so the absent member is **not** reported as `missingGhostMember` drift. **(4) Exclude from MITAA export:** a de-brothered record is **excluded from the MITAA export (D90) regardless of `allowShareWithMITAA`** — a de-brothered person's data is shared with no one outside staff. **Reversible:** because de-brothering can be mistaken or appealed, the consent/identity/verification state is **snapshotted at mark-time and restored on un-de-brothering**, which **re-creates the Ghost member via the D96 Ghost-first create path** and restores consent — exactly symmetric with the un-decease snapshot/restore (D80). De-brothered and deceased are **orthogonal flags**; if both are set, hide-from-brothers wins for the brother role (staff see strikethrough plus the In-Memoriam treatment). Records to `DATABASE-SCHEMA §3`/`§8`/`§9`, `API-SPEC §2`/`§3`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.4`/`§2.1`/`§5.1`, `PRD §4`/`§5.6`/`§5.7`, `USER-MANUAL §7`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phases 4/5); reuses D80, D90, D96, D98; extends D5/D16/D19/D82 and D99; cites D22, D97.
**Why:** De-brothering is rare but must be airtight — the person loses all access (Book and Ghost) and all visibility to peers, with no data leaking through any egress (peer view, export, or MITAA). Reusing machinery Book already has — the delete-in-Ghost path, the read projection, the snapshot/restore pattern, and the existing tolerance for a Book profile with no Ghost member (the no-email/unidentified brothers, C15/D20) — keeps the exception cheap: the genuinely new code is a projection branch, a login-deny check, one audit-exception rule, and the strikethrough. Deletion-in-Ghost was chosen over fake-email neutralization because the latter fights the email-as-identity and one-namespace-uniqueness invariants the design just hardened, leaves a live neutered Ghost member, and needs an extra unsubscribe step the delete avoids. Reversibility is the safety net against an admin error or a successful appeal, and it falls out for free from the existing un-decease and Ghost-create paths. (Forrest's call on both the revocation mechanism and reversibility.)

*Later updated by: D124 (the projection-omission mechanism reused for the non-punitive "Unlisted" privacy hide — a record present-but-hidden-from-peers, the member fully retained; deliberately contrasted with de-brothering's strike-through and Ghost-member deletion).*

### D116 — A persistent privacy-notice link in a global page footer (pins the position D77 left open)
D77 requires Book to link PBE's public privacy notice (`https://pbe400.org/privacy/`, which already names Mixpanel, MITAA, and Ghost) from the **login / landing** surface so it precedes first-sign-in auto-provisioning, but placed no link *inside* the authenticated app. This amendment adds a **persistent, global footer** at the bottom of **every** Book page, carrying a link to that **same** privacy notice — confirming Forrest's suspicion that the *need* was captured (D77) but not the *position*. It is deliberately quiet — bottom-of-page, low-emphasis — present so the notice is always reachable, not so it is prominent. No new notice is authored: the footer and the D77 login/landing link point at the one PBE privacy notice (which, hosted on the Ghost site at `pbe400.org`, is the "Ghost privacy policy" the amendment names). Records to `PRD §5.2`, `USER-MANUAL §8`, `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS §D`; complements D77.
**Why:** A notice reachable only from the login surface leaves a signed-in brother with no standing way back to it; a persistent footer link is the conventional, unobtrusive fix and costs nothing. Linking the single existing PBE notice (rather than inventing a separate "Book privacy policy") avoids a second document to keep in sync.

### D117 — An optional admin-set system banner across all pages, mirroring Ghost's announcement bar
Book gains an **optional system-message banner** across the top of every page, **settable and clearable by administrators only**, functionally and visually mirroring Ghost's announcement bar so the composite system feels of a piece — though the two are **independent**: a Book banner never propagates to Ghost or vice versa (different messages at different times). It is a single global **singleton** — a small `systemBanner` config document holding the message text, an on/off state, and a severity level (info / warning) — written through the one live instance by an admin (no online-bulk-write concern under D83/D100), read by every client, and rendered site-wide. It is **not per-user dismissible** (it persists until an admin clears it, matching Ghost), and the severity selects styling. It is **distinct from the maintenance page (D118)**: the banner is an in-app message shown while Book is *fully operational*, whereas the maintenance page replaces the app when the system is *down*. Records to `DATABASE-SCHEMA §6.3`, `API-SPEC §10`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.3`, `PRD §5.2`/`§5.8`, `USER-MANUAL §7`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phases 1/5); distinct from D118.
**Why:** PBE occasionally needs to tell every Book user something at once — an upcoming maintenance window, a data-cleanup notice, a reunion reminder — without emailing the brotherhood; an admin-controlled banner is the standard, low-effort surface, and matching Ghost's banner keeps the two halves coherent. A single admin-written config document fits the single-instance, no-bulk-write model exactly. Persisting until cleared (rather than per-user dismiss) matches Ghost and keeps behavior obvious for the one or two admins who use it.

### D118 — Down-for-maintenance / outage page: an edge static page for planned downtime plus an SPA auto-fallback for unplanned outages
Book needs a true "**down for maintenance, check back later**" full-page state — not the in-app banner (D117) but the case where the *system itself* is unavailable — covering both **planned** downtime (an operator takes Book down: the offline restore of D100/D101, a migration, a deploy) and **unplanned** outage (the backend is broken or unreachable). Because the backend may be the very thing that is down, no single backend-served flag suffices; the resolution is a two-part mechanism covering the full {fresh-load vs. cached-SPA} × {planned vs. unplanned} matrix. **(1) Edge / Hosting static maintenance page (planned):** an operator **script** swaps a static `maintenance.html` in at the Firebase Hosting / edge layer, served independently of Cloud Run, so a visitor loading Book fresh during planned downtime gets an honest static page even though the backend is stopped. (Forrest's framing: the "maintenance flag" is concretely an edge static-page swap, by script — his stated preference over an admin-page toggle.) **(2) SPA auto-fallback (unplanned, and cached-SPA-during-planned):** when the cached SPA shell is already loaded (D73) and its API calls fail, time out, or return a maintenance signal, the SPA **renders a maintenance/outage fallback itself** rather than a broken UI — covering the "ideal: it shows up when something is broken" wish for already-open and returning tabs. To distinguish the two, the backend (when reachable) returns a **distinct maintenance-flavored `503`** with `Retry-After` during planned downtime, so the cached SPA can show "down for maintenance" copy rather than a generic "can't reach the server" message; an unannounced hard failure falls back to generic-outage copy. This **generalizes the "hard maintenance" state §6.3 already invokes for restore** (D100/D101) into a first-class, script-triggered operator capability plus a client-side safety net. Records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §6.3`, `API-SPEC §1.5`/`§10`, `PRD §5.2`, `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS §B`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phase 5); extends D100/D101; cites D73; distinct from D117.
**Why:** "The system is down" must be communicable even when the backend can't talk — which is exactly why a single app-served flag is insufficient and an edge static page (independent of Cloud Run) is the honest planned-downtime mechanism. The cached SPA, which D73 guarantees a returning user already holds, is the only thing positioned to react to an *unplanned* outage, so letting it self-render a fallback is the cheapest way to cover the "shows up when something breaks" case. The maintenance-flavored `503` is a small touch that lets the same fallback distinguish a deliberate outage from a crash. Script triggering (over an admin-page toggle) is Forrest's call — it keeps the planned path working when the app is intentionally down, and matches how restore and migration already run offline.

### D119 — A cold-start / load "Loading…" overlay, shown only past a short threshold, with a wake-the-server reassurance line
Because Cloud Run scale-to-zero makes **cold starts common** (D83/D85) and a meaningful fraction of brothers are on **slow links** (D73–D76), the first load can take several seconds at the worst moment — the first click-through after a newsletter send. Book shows a **"Loading…" overlay with an animated spinner** during the gap between app open and the dataset being ready in the browser, **but only if** that gap exceeds a short threshold (~500 ms), so a fast load shows nothing. If the wait runs longer (~3–4 s), the overlay adds a **reassurance line** ("Waking the server — this can take a few seconds…") so a cold start reads as expected behaviour, not a hang. It is dismissed once the bulk dataset has arrived and the Directory can render (the D110 Web Worker lights up fuzzy/phonetic search slightly later, which is fine — exact/substring matching is available immediately). Purely a frontend affordance over the existing bulk load (`GET /api/profiles`) and the cold-start path; no backend change. Records to `PRD §5.5`/`§5.6`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.6`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phases 1/7); serves D73–D76; cites D83, D85, D110.
**Why:** A blank or frozen screen during a multi-second cold start is exactly what the slow-connection, older-hardware audience would read as "broken"; a threshold-gated spinner plus an honest "waking the server" line turns dead time into legible feedback at zero backend cost. Gating on a threshold avoids a flash of spinner on fast loads; the reassurance line specifically addresses the cold-start latency the rest of the architecture deliberately accepts as the price of scale-to-zero.

### D120 — Email-bounce reporting lives in the offline audit, not in Book's UI
Ghost logs when a PBE News email to a brother bounces, and an adjacent project already retrieves it: `ghost-member-export/export-bounces.js` joins Ghost's `email_failed_event` member-events to members (`member_id`→email) and aggregates per brother to `email / bounce_count / last_bounce_at / last_bounce_newsletter` (working around Ghost's `/admin/emails/` 403 via the posts endpoint, and skipping events for hard-deleted members). The amendment's first choice — a live "BOUNCING" warning *inside* Book (on the brother's own pages, and on directory/profile views for staff) — is **dropped as too much work for the value** (it would need a synced report-only field, UI in several surfaces, and refresh plumbing the scale-to-zero design has no live path for). Instead, **bounce reporting is built into the offline Ghost audit**: each time the reconciliation/sync audit (D99) is run, it **also produces a bounce report** — reusing the `export-bounces.js` join — as an **out-of-band artifact for the admin** (logged and/or written as a CSV), and **nothing bounce-related is rendered in Book's UI** (no per-user banner, no directory/profile indicator, no Admin-page bounce panel). The bounce-report generator joins the ongoing operational side-tools inventory. Records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §5.1`, `PRE-LAUNCH-TOOLS §B`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phase 5); reuses the existing `export-bounces.js`; relates to D99.
**Why:** Bounce status is operationally useful but not urgent — PBE News is bi-annual — and Book's scale-to-zero design has no live-polling path, so a real-time in-UI warning would be disproportionate plumbing for a signal an admin can act on from a periodic report. The join already exists and is proven in `export-bounces.js`, so folding it into the audit's outputs is nearly free, and keeping it out of Book's UI honors both the "abandon the in-app display" call and the logs-carry-names-not-a-live-surface discipline. (Forrest's call to descope the in-Book display and move it to the audit.)

### D121 — "Report a bug" is a top-bar link that files a stored report, not an email *(Linear: OFC-172)*
Book gains a visible, easy **"Report a bug"** affordance at the top of every page, **next to the user's avatar / menu** (the §5.2 global-controls cluster). On the delivery mechanism — which carries the security weight Forrest flagged (email as a DoS / header-injection / spam-amplification vector) — the chosen design is an **in-app form that POSTs to the backend, which stores the report and writes a structured audit/log entry (D61); there is no outbound email**. The report captures the submitter (already an authenticated brother — Book is members-only, so this is never an anonymous-internet surface), the current page/route, a free-text description, and useful client context; the admin reviews the queue via the Admin page or the logs (the log-reader path, D61/D91). The endpoint is **rate-limited (D86)** and size-capped, and its free text is treated as untrusted (never interpolated into a dangerous sink, never emailed). Storing-plus-logging over emailing **removes the email-header-injection and amplification surface entirely** — the strongest answer to the DoS concern — at the cost that the admin checks a queue rather than receiving a push. Records to `DATABASE-SCHEMA §6.4`, `API-SPEC §10`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.3`/`§6.1`, `PRD §5.2`, `USER-MANUAL §7`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phase 5); cites D61, D86, D91.
**Why:** A first-class bug-report path is worth having for an older, less technical user base that won't open GitHub issues, and placing it by the avatar makes it findable. Storing the report rather than emailing it is the security-respecting choice Forrest's note pointed at: it keeps the one or two admins' inbox out of the attack surface entirely, leans on controls Book already has (rate limits, structured logging, the constrained log-reader agent), and is bounded anyway because every submitter is a known, authenticated brother. The convenience cost — no push notification — is small at Book's scale and can be added later as a rate-limited digest if wanted. (Forrest's call to store-and-log rather than email.)

---

## Early-feedback design changes (2026-06-24)

*Five changes from early reader feedback, discussed before adoption. While propagating them, a consistency audit (requested by Forrest) found that two Session-3 privacy decisions — **D89** (`allowShareWithMITAA` defaults `false`/opt-in) and **D93** (`shareEmergency` defaults `false`; `shareSpousePartner` toggle added; `spousePartnerName` becomes toggle-class) — had never been propagated into `DATABASE-SCHEMA.md` or `PRD §5.7.3`, though `USER-MANUAL §4`/§8 carried them correctly. That gap is **corrected in this pass** (the docs now match the decisions as written; D89/D93 themselves are unchanged).*

### D122 — Deceased lifespan: optional birth/death-year fields, a `b.`/`d.` dated display, and a guided mark-deceased entry
Two optional fields are added to `DeceasedInfo`: **`birthYear?`** (the year of birth, typically looked up from an obituary at mark-deceased time) and **`deathYear?`** (a *coarse fallback* used only when the full `dateOfDeath` is unknown). The two death representations are **mutually exclusive**: when a full `dateOfDeath` is present the death year is derived from it and `deathYear` is rejected on write; `deathYear` exists only to record a year-but-not-a-full-date death. Both are **public-class** but **render only when the brother is deceased**. The Profile's In Memoriam banner (PRD §5.7.7) shows a **lifespan line** in memorial `b.`/`d.` notation, which handles every partial case without a dangling dash: both years → **"1940–2024"** (a non-breaking **en**-dash); death year only (no birth) → **"d. 2024"**; birth year only (no death year/date) → **nothing** (an open "1940–" on a known-deceased brother reads as still-living); neither → nothing. The exact `dateOfDeath` still displays as a detail field when present, independent of the lifespan line. **`birthYear` is collected only at or after mark-deceased — never for a living brother** — so a living person's birth year (an age / identity-theft input) is never stored or exposed. Both fields are settable/editable by **managers and admins**, consistent with the other deceased fields (PRD §4); they are kept verbatim through an un-mark/re-mark cycle (D80 snapshot semantics) and are not displayed while not deceased. Validation: plausible year ranges (birth `[1850, currentYear]`, death `≥ birthYear` and `≤ currentYear`). **Entry UX (amends D49):** the soft mark-deceased confirmation stays a *pure confirmation*; on confirm, the deceased fields — now **five** (`dateOfDeath`, `deathYear`, `birthYear`, `obituaryUrl`, `inMemoriamUrl`) — reveal inline in the edit form **and the form auto-focuses/scrolls to them** so the manager records what's known immediately, saved with the normal page Save. A modal data-entry dialog was rejected (it would duplicate the inline fields, which must exist anyway for later edits — an obituary or birth year often surfaces weeks after a death); an always-visible-but-disabled block was rejected (greyed-out "Date of death" on every living brother's edit page is clutter, and faintly grim). Records to `DATABASE-SCHEMA §3.2`/`§3.3`/`§8`/`§10`, `PRD §5.7.6`/`§5.7.7`, `USER-MANUAL §3`, `VISUAL-DESIGN-BRIEF`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phase 4). Amends D49; updates the "three deceased fields" count to five.
**Why:** A memorial lifespan is a small, dignified addition that fits the In Memoriam moment the design already commits to giving "real care," at almost no cost. The `b.`/`d.` convention is the lasting one and degrades gracefully when only part of the data is known. Deriving the death year from the full date (rather than a free-standing field that can contradict it) keeps one source of truth. Restricting `birthYear` to deceased records sidesteps the only real privacy objection — a living person's age — entirely. Reveal-and-focus reuses the inline fields the page needs regardless, so it captures the data immediately without a second, separately-validated form.

### D123 — Name Search also matches common given-name nicknames (a curated diminutives expansion)
Name Search (D35) is extended so common Western **diminutives match in both directions** — "Bill" finds "William," "Tom" finds "Thomas," and vice-versa. A **curated bidirectional diminutives dictionary** (a small bundled static asset, e.g. an open given-name/nickname dataset) expands the **first / middle / full-legal given-name tokens** into their nickname group at index-build time, and expands the **query token** the same way; Fuse.js typo tolerance and talisman phonetic matching (D35/D66) then run over the expanded set. This is purely additive to the existing tokenize-at-load pipeline and is built inside the **D110 Web Worker** index, so it needs **zero per-brother data entry** and adds no main-thread work. It is **config-flagged and A/B-tunable** against the realistic test dataset, exactly like the Double-Metaphone↔Beider-Morse experiment (D66), and **ships on by default**. Over-matching (e.g. "Jack" → both "John" and "Jackson") is acceptable given AND-across-query-tokens, the small result set, and match-highlighting; the layer can be tuned or disabled if it proves noisy. It covers English/Western names only, but does not harm non-Western names (phonetic matching still covers those). Extends D35.
**Why:** Diminutive↔formal is a *semantic* name mapping that neither typo tolerance (small edit distance) nor phonetic matching (sound-alike) catches — "Bill"/"William" are neither — so the current pipeline misses an extremely common real-world lookup for a directory whose entire job is findability. A curated dictionary fixes it with no data-entry burden and slots into the existing client-side, Web-Worker search with no architecture change; flagging it keeps it tunable and reversible.

### D124 — "Unlisted": a self-service whole-record privacy hide, distinct from de-brothering
A brother may make his record **Unlisted** — removed from the directory **for peers**, while he **remains a full member** in every other respect (he still signs in, still receives PBE News, and still flows to MITAA per his own consent). A new boolean **`unlisted`** (default `false`) governs it. It is **primarily self-service**: the **owner** sets it on his own record, like the other privacy toggles; a **staff-assisted** change is **admin-only** (managers cannot alter another brother's privacy/consent — consistent with §9 / D19 / D106). Enforcement reuses the de-brothering projection machinery (D115): an unlisted record is **omitted from the brother-role `GET /api/profiles`** entirely and a brother who requests it by id gets **404/403**, while **managers and admins still receive it**, flagged, and render an **"UNLISTED" badge** — visually **distinct from de-brothering's strike-through** (present-but-private, not expelled). Unlisting is **orthogonal to every other sharing control**: it governs **directory listing only**, so `allowShareWithMITAA`, the per-field share toggles, and newsletter consent are all unaffected — an unlisted brother who has opted into MITAA still flows to MITAA, and the identity/public-death always-flow to MITAA (D89) still applies. This is the deliberate contrast with **`debrothered`** (D115), which is punitive, admin-only, deletes the Ghost member, denies sign-in, and drops the record from MITAA. The Profile privacy section names the control in plain language with the D113 inline-consequence treatment ("You won't appear in the directory for other brothers; managers and administrators can still see your record"). The directory's resting bias stays **listed by default** (an empty directory serves no one), and a peer's existing star on a now-unlisted brother simply resolves to nothing for that peer. Records to `DATABASE-SCHEMA §3.1`/`§3.3`/`§9`/`§10`, `PRD §4.1`/`§4.2`/`§5.6.5`/`§5.7.3`, `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.4`, `API-SPEC §1.3`/`§3`, `USER-MANUAL §5`/`§8`, `VISUAL-DESIGN-BRIEF`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phases 2–4). Contrasts with D115; reuses its projection mechanism.
**Why:** A brother may want to stay a member — keep his data current for staff, keep getting the newsletter — without being visible to peers (privacy preference, personal safety, a sensitive profession). The per-field toggles can't express that; a whole-record hide can. Reusing the de-brothering projection means no new mechanism, only a second reason a record is projected away — but the two must stay sharply distinct (membership retained vs. expelled; badge vs. strike-through), or staff would misread one as the other. Keeping it orthogonal to MITAA and the per-field toggles avoids conflating directory presence with separate, independently-governed consents.

### D125 — Book sessions and the login nonce are persisted in Firestore so cold starts don't force re-login
The server-side **session record** (ENGINEERING-DESIGN §2.3) and the single-use **`state` login nonce** (§2.1) are stored in **Firestore** — a small `sessions` collection (and `authNonces`), each with a native **TTL** on an `expiresAt` field — **not** in instance memory. The problem this closes: under scale-to-zero (D83) the single instance shuts down whenever idle, and a *browsing* brother makes **no backend calls** after the initial load (search/filter/sort are client-side; images come from the CDN), so the instance routinely scales to zero **while a brother is still reading the directory**. If the session lived only in memory, **every cold start would invalidate every session** and bounce the next action back through the bridge — forcing a full SPA reload and re-download of the whole dataset over a slow link (the very cohort the design protects), and turning D109's deliberately-rare mid-edit re-auth into a constant event. Firestore-backing **decouples session validity from instance lifecycle** while preserving exactly why §2.3 chose a server-side record over a self-signed JWT: the **4-hour absolute cap and revocation stay server-enforced** (the cap rides in the stored record). The nonce gets the same treatment so a cold start landing **between** sign-in initiation and the callback cannot fail the nonce check. A read-through **in-memory cache** keeps warm requests free; only cold starts pay a Firestore read. Genuine expiry still occurs (the 4-hour cap; removal from Ghost) and is handled as today — a clear **"Your session has expired — please sign in again"** message (a *silent* redirect when the brother is still a Ghost member; a denial when removed) — and **re-auth / session-expiry events are logged** (§6.1 audit + Mixpanel) so the real frequency is measurable; Firestore-backing should drop that frequency to the true cap rate. Records to `ENGINEERING-DESIGN §1.6`/`§2.1`/`§2.3`/`§2.7`/`§6.1`, `DATABASE-SCHEMA §1`/`§6`, `API-SPEC §2`/`§1.5`, `PRD §5.2`, `CODING-PROJECT-PLAN` (Phase 1). Amends §2.1/§2.3/§2.7 and D109's frequency assumption; cites D83, D87, D22, D109.
**Why:** The design already chose a server-side session record (over a JWT) precisely so the cap and revocation are enforced server-side — but left *where* the record lives unpinned, and the naive in-memory implementation is silently incompatible with scale-to-zero, which the architecture elsewhere calls out as making cold starts common. Storing the record (and the nonce) in Firestore is the faithful realization of §2.3's intent, costs almost nothing at ~700 users with TTL cleanup, and restores D109's design assumption that a `401` is a rare event rather than a routine one. It is a root-cause fix; the user-facing message and the logging are the secondary, still-useful halves for the genuine-expiry case that remains.

*Later updated by: D126 (a browsing brother's image fetches now reach the app rather than a CDN, but reading still has quiet, call-free stretches in which the single instance scales to zero — so Firestore-persisted sessions remain load-bearing).*

---

## Cost re-evaluation (2026-06-25)

*One change from a cost review during the Phase 1a infrastructure planning, discussed before adoption. Standing up the staging environment surfaced that D23's media-serving choice silently requires an external HTTPS load balancer — a fixed ~$18–25/month charge, in production, regardless of traffic — which is the one component that breaks Book's otherwise ~$0 idle floor (D83). That fixed cost was never weighed when D23 was made, so D23's serving mechanism was deliberately reopened.*
